"235"	"PREFACE"
"236"	"Most of the adventures recorded in this book really occurred; one or two"
"237"	"were experiences of my own, the rest those of boys who were schoolmates"
"238"	"of mine. Huck Finn is drawn from life; Tom Sawyer also, but not from an"
"239"	"individual--he is a combination of the characteristics of three boys whom"
"240"	"I knew, and therefore belongs to the composite order of architecture."
"241"	"The odd superstitions touched upon were all prevalent among children and"
"242"	"slaves in the West at the period of this story--that is to say, thirty or"
"243"	"forty years ago."
"244"	"Although my book is intended mainly for the entertainment of boys and"
"245"	"girls, I hope it will not be shunned by men and women on that account,"
"246"	"for part of my plan has been to try to pleasantly remind adults of what"
"247"	"they once were themselves, and of how they felt and thought and talked,"
"248"	"and what queer enterprises they sometimes engaged in."
"249"	"THE AUTHOR."
"250"	"HARTFORD, 1876."
"251"	"CHAPTER I"
"252"	"“TOM!”"
"253"	"No answer."
"254"	"“TOM!”"
"255"	"No answer."
"256"	"“What’s gone with that boy,  I wonder? You TOM!”"
"257"	"No answer."
"258"	"The old lady pulled her spectacles down and looked over them about the"
"259"	"room; then she put them up and looked out under them. She seldom or"
"260"	"never looked _through_ them for so small a thing as a boy; they were"
"261"	"her state pair, the pride of her heart, and were built for “style,” not"
"262"	"service--she could have seen through a pair of stove-lids just as well."
"263"	"She looked perplexed for a moment, and then said, not fiercely, but"
"264"	"still loud enough for the furniture to hear:"
"265"	"“Well, I lay if I get hold of you I’ll--”"
"266"	"She did not finish, for by this time she was bending down and punching"
"267"	"under the bed with the broom, and so she needed breath to punctuate the"
"268"	"punches with. She resurrected nothing but the cat."
"269"	"“I never did see the beat of that boy!”"
"270"	"She went to the open door and stood in it and looked out among the"
"271"	"tomato vines and “jimpson” weeds that constituted the garden. No Tom. So"
"272"	"she lifted up her voice at an angle calculated for distance and shouted:"
"273"	"“Y-o-u-u TOM!”"
"274"	"There was a slight noise behind her and she turned just in time to seize"
"275"	"a small boy by the slack of his roundabout and arrest his flight."
"276"	"“There! I might ‘a’ thought of that closet. What you been doing in"
"277"	"there?”"
"278"	"“Nothing.”"
"279"	"“Nothing! Look at your hands. And look at your mouth. What _is_ that"
"280"	"truck?”"
"281"	"“I don’t know, aunt.”"
"282"	"“Well, I know. It’s jam--that’s what it is. Forty times I’ve said if you"
"283"	"didn’t let that jam alone I’d skin you. Hand me that switch.”"
"284"	"The switch hovered in the air--the peril was desperate--"
"285"	"“My! Look behind you, aunt!”"
"286"	"The old lady whirled round, and snatched her skirts out of danger."
"287"	"The lad fled on the instant, scrambled up the high board-fence, and"
"288"	"disappeared over it."
"289"	"His aunt Polly stood surprised a moment, and then broke into a gentle"
"290"	"laugh."
"291"	"“Hang the boy, can’t I never learn anything? Ain’t he played me tricks"
"292"	"enough like that for me to be looking out for him by this time? But old"
"293"	"fools is the biggest fools there is. Can’t learn an old dog new tricks,"
"294"	"as the saying is. But my goodness, he never plays them alike, two days,"
"295"	"and how is a body to know what’s coming? He ‘pears to know just how long"
"296"	"he can torment me before I get my dander up, and he knows if he can make"
"297"	"out to put me off for a minute or make me laugh, it’s all down again and"
"298"	"I can’t hit him a lick. I ain’t doing my duty by that boy, and that’s"
"299"	"the Lord’s truth, goodness knows. Spare the rod and spile the child,"
"300"	"as the Good Book says. I’m a laying up sin and suffering for us both,"
"301"	"I know. He’s full of the Old Scratch, but laws-a-me! he’s my own"
"302"	"dead sister’s boy, poor thing, and I ain’t got the heart to lash him,"
"303"	"somehow. Every time I let him off, my conscience does hurt me so, and"
"304"	"every time I hit him my old heart most breaks. Well-a-well, man that is"
"305"	"born of woman is of few days and full of trouble, as the Scripture"
"306"	"says, and I reckon it’s so. He’ll play hookey this evening, * and [*"
"307"	"Southwestern for “afternoon”] I’ll just be obleeged to make him work,"
"308"	"tomorrow, to punish him. It’s mighty hard to make him work Saturdays,"
"309"	"when all the boys is having holiday, but he hates work more than he"
"310"	"hates anything else, and I’ve _got_ to do some of my duty by him, or"
"311"	"I’ll be the ruination of the child.”"
"312"	"Tom did play hookey, and he had a very good time. He got back home"
"313"	"barely in season to help Jim, the small colored boy, saw next-day’s wood"
"314"	"and split the kindlings before supper--at least he was there in time"
"315"	"to tell his adventures to Jim while Jim did three-fourths of the work."
"316"	"Tom’s younger brother (or rather half-brother) Sid was already through"
"317"	"with his part of the work (picking up chips), for he was a quiet boy,"
"318"	"and had no adventurous, trouble-some ways."
"319"	"While Tom was eating his supper, and stealing sugar as opportunity"
"320"	"offered, Aunt Polly asked him questions that were full of guile, and"
"321"	"very deep--for she wanted to trap him into damaging revealments. Like"
"322"	"many other simple-hearted souls, it was her pet vanity to believe she"
"323"	"was endowed with a talent for dark and mysterious diplomacy, and she"
"324"	"loved to contemplate her most transparent devices as marvels of low"
"325"	"cunning. Said she:"
"326"	"“Tom, it was middling warm in school, warn’t it?”"
"327"	"“Yes’m.”"
"328"	"“Powerful warm, warn’t it?”"
"329"	"“Yes’m.”"
"330"	"“Didn’t you want to go in a-swimming, Tom?”"
"331"	"A bit of a scare shot through Tom--a touch of uncomfortable suspicion. He"
"332"	"searched Aunt Polly’s face, but it told him nothing. So he said:"
"333"	"“No’m--well, not very much.”"
"334"	"The old lady reached out her hand and felt Tom’s shirt, and said:"
"335"	"“But you ain’t too warm now, though.” And it flattered her to reflect"
"336"	"that she had discovered that the shirt was dry without anybody knowing"
"337"	"that that was what she had in her mind. But in spite of her, Tom knew"
"338"	"where the wind lay, now. So he forestalled what might be the next move:"
"339"	"“Some of us pumped on our heads--mine’s damp yet. See?”"
"340"	"Aunt Polly was vexed to think she had overlooked that bit of"
"341"	"circumstantial evidence, and missed a trick. Then she had a new"
"342"	"inspiration:"
"343"	"“Tom, you didn’t have to undo your shirt collar where I sewed it, to"
"344"	"pump on your head, did you? Unbutton your jacket!”"
"345"	"The trouble vanished out of Tom’s face. He opened his jacket. His shirt"
"346"	"collar was securely sewed."
"347"	"“Bother! Well, go ‘long with you. I’d made sure you’d played hookey"
"348"	"and been a-swimming. But I forgive ye, Tom. I reckon you’re a kind of a"
"349"	"singed cat, as the saying is--better’n you look. _This_ time.”"
"350"	"She was half sorry her sagacity had miscarried, and half glad that Tom"
"351"	"had stumbled into obedient conduct for once."
"352"	"But Sidney said:"
"353"	"“Well, now, if I didn’t think you sewed his collar with white thread,"
"354"	"but it’s black.”"
"355"	"“Why, I did sew it with white! Tom!”"
"356"	"But Tom did not wait for the rest. As he went out at the door he said:"
"357"	"“Siddy, I’ll lick you for that.”"
"358"	"In a safe place Tom examined two large needles which were thrust into"
"359"	"the lapels of his jacket, and had thread bound about them--one needle"
"360"	"carried white thread and the other black. He said:"
"361"	"“She’d never noticed if it hadn’t been for Sid. Confound it! sometimes"
"362"	"she sews it with white, and sometimes she sews it with black. I wish to"
"363"	"gee-miny she’d stick to one or t’other--I can’t keep the run of ‘em. But"
"364"	"I bet you I’ll lam Sid for that. I’ll learn him!”"
"365"	"He was not the Model Boy of the village. He knew the model boy very well"
"366"	"though--and loathed him."
"367"	"Within two minutes, or even less, he had forgotten all his troubles. Not"
"368"	"because his troubles were one whit less heavy and bitter to him than a"
"369"	"man’s are to a man, but because a new and powerful interest bore"
"370"	"them down and drove them out of his mind for the time--just as men’s"
"371"	"misfortunes are forgotten in the excitement of new enterprises. This new"
"372"	"interest was a valued novelty in whistling, which he had just acquired"
"373"	"from a negro, and he was suffering to practise it un-disturbed. It"
"374"	"consisted in a peculiar bird-like turn, a sort of liquid warble,"
"375"	"produced by touching the tongue to the roof of the mouth at short"
"376"	"intervals in the midst of the music--the reader probably remembers how to"
"377"	"do it, if he has ever been a boy. Diligence and attention soon gave him"
"378"	"the knack of it, and he strode down the street with his mouth full of"
"379"	"harmony and his soul full of gratitude. He felt much as an astronomer"
"380"	"feels who has discovered a new planet--no doubt, as far as strong, deep,"
"381"	"unalloyed pleasure is concerned, the advantage was with the boy, not the"
"382"	"astronomer."
"383"	"The summer evenings were long. It was not dark, yet. Presently Tom"
"384"	"checked his whistle. A stranger was before him--a boy a shade larger"
"385"	"than himself. A new-comer of any age or either sex was an im-pressive"
"386"	"curiosity in the poor little shabby village of St. Petersburg. This boy"
"387"	"was well dressed, too--well dressed on a week-day. This was simply as"
"388"	"astounding. His cap was a dainty thing, his close-buttoned blue cloth"
"389"	"roundabout was new and natty, and so were his pantaloons. He had shoes"
"390"	"on--and it was only Friday. He even wore a necktie, a bright bit of"
"391"	"ribbon. He had a citified air about him that ate into Tom’s vitals. The"
"392"	"more Tom stared at the splendid marvel, the higher he turned up his nose"
"393"	"at his finery and the shabbier and shabbier his own outfit seemed to"
"394"	"him to grow. Neither boy spoke. If one moved, the other moved--but only"
"395"	"sidewise, in a circle; they kept face to face and eye to eye all the"
"396"	"time. Finally Tom said:"
"397"	"“I can lick you!”"
"398"	"“I’d like to see you try it.”"
"399"	"“Well, I can do it.”"
"400"	"“No you can’t, either.”"
"401"	"“Yes I can.”"
"402"	"“No you can’t.”"
"403"	"“I can.”"
"404"	"“You can’t.”"
"405"	"“Can!”"
"406"	"“Can’t!”"
"407"	"An uncomfortable pause. Then Tom said:"
"408"	"“What’s your name?”"
"409"	"“‘Tisn’t any of your business, maybe.”"
"410"	"“Well I ‘low I’ll _make_ it my business.”"
"411"	"“Well why don’t you?”"
"412"	"“If you say much, I will.”"
"413"	"“Much--much--_much_. There now.”"
"414"	"“Oh, you think you’re mighty smart, _don’t_ you? I could lick you with"
"415"	"one hand tied behind me, if I wanted to.”"
"416"	"“Well why don’t you _do_ it? You _say_ you can do it.”"
"417"	"“Well I _will_, if you fool with me.”"
"418"	"“Oh yes--I’ve seen whole families in the same fix.”"
"419"	"“Smarty! You think you’re _some_, now, _don’t_ you? Oh, what a hat!”"
"420"	"“You can lump that hat if you don’t like it. I dare you to knock it"
"421"	"off--and anybody that’ll take a dare will suck eggs.”"
"422"	"“You’re a liar!”"
"423"	"“You’re another.”"
"424"	"“You’re a fighting liar and dasn’t take it up.”"
"425"	"“Aw--take a walk!”"
"426"	"“Say--if you give me much more of your sass I’ll take and bounce a rock"
"427"	"off’n your head.”"
"428"	"“Oh, of _course_ you will.”"
"429"	"“Well I _will_.”"
"430"	"“Well why don’t you _do_ it then? What do you keep _saying_ you will"
"431"	"for? Why don’t you _do_ it? It’s because you’re afraid.”"
"432"	"“I _ain’t_ afraid.”"
"433"	"“You are.”"
"434"	"“I ain’t.”"
"435"	"“You are.”"
"436"	"Another pause, and more eying and sidling around each other. Presently"
"437"	"they were shoulder to shoulder. Tom said:"
"438"	"“Get away from here!”"
"439"	"“Go away yourself!”"
"440"	"“I won’t.”"
"441"	"“I won’t either.”"
"442"	"So they stood, each with a foot placed at an angle as a brace, and both"
"443"	"shoving with might and main, and glowering at each other with hate. But"
"444"	"neither could get an advantage. After struggling till both were hot and"
"445"	"flushed, each relaxed his strain with watchful caution, and Tom said:"
"446"	"“You’re a coward and a pup. I’ll tell my big brother on you, and he can"
"447"	"thrash you with his little finger, and I’ll make him do it, too.”"
"448"	"“What do I care for your big brother? I’ve got a brother that’s bigger"
"449"	"than he is--and what’s more, he can throw him over that fence, too.”"
"450"	" [Both brothers were imaginary.]"
"451"	"“That’s a lie.”"
"452"	"“_Your_ saying so don’t make it so.”"
"453"	"Tom drew a line in the dust with his big toe, and said:"
"454"	"“I dare you to step over that, and I’ll lick you till you can’t stand"
"455"	"up. Anybody that’ll take a dare will steal sheep.”"
"456"	"The new boy stepped over promptly, and said:"
"457"	"“Now you said you’d do it, now let’s see you do it.”"
"458"	"“Don’t you crowd me now; you better look out.”"
"459"	"“Well, you _said_ you’d do it--why don’t you do it?”"
"460"	"“By jingo! for two cents I _will_ do it.”"
"461"	"The new boy took two broad coppers out of his pocket and held them out"
"462"	"with derision. Tom struck them to the ground. In an instant both boys"
"463"	"were rolling and tumbling in the dirt, gripped together like cats; and"
"464"	"for the space of a minute they tugged and tore at each other’s hair and"
"465"	"clothes, punched and scratched each other’s nose, and covered themselves"
"466"	"with dust and glory. Presently the confusion took form, and through the"
"467"	"fog of battle Tom appeared, seated astride the new boy, and pounding him"
"468"	"with his fists. “Holler ‘nuff!” said he."
"469"	"The boy only struggled to free himself. He was crying--mainly from rage."
"470"	"“Holler ‘nuff!”--and the pounding went on."
"471"	"At last the stranger got out a smothered “‘Nuff!” and Tom let him up and"
"472"	"said:"
"473"	"“Now that’ll learn you. Better look out who you’re fooling with next"
"474"	"time.”"
"475"	"The new boy went off brushing the dust from his clothes, sobbing,"
"476"	"snuffling, and occasionally looking back and shaking his head and"
"477"	"threatening what he would do to Tom the “next time he caught him out.”"
"478"	" To which Tom responded with jeers, and started off in high feather, and"
"479"	"as soon as his back was turned the new boy snatched up a stone, threw it"
"480"	"and hit him between the shoulders and then turned tail and ran like"
"481"	"an antelope. Tom chased the traitor home, and thus found out where he"
"482"	"lived. He then held a position at the gate for some time, daring the"
"483"	"enemy to come outside, but the enemy only made faces at him through the"
"484"	"window and declined. At last the enemy’s mother appeared, and called Tom"
"485"	"a bad, vicious, vulgar child, and ordered him away. So he went away; but"
"486"	"he said he “‘lowed” to “lay” for that boy."
"487"	"He got home pretty late that night, and when he climbed cautiously in"
"488"	"at the window, he uncovered an ambuscade, in the person of his aunt; and"
"489"	"when she saw the state his clothes were in her resolution to turn his"
"490"	"Saturday holiday into captivity at hard labor became adamantine in its"
"491"	"firmness."
"492"	"CHAPTER II"
"493"	"SATURDAY morning was come, and all the summer world was bright and"
"494"	"fresh, and brimming with life. There was a song in every heart; and if"
"495"	"the heart was young the music issued at the lips. There was cheer in"
"496"	"every face and a spring in every step. The locust-trees were in bloom"
"497"	"and the fragrance of the blossoms filled the air. Cardiff Hill, beyond"
"498"	"the village and above it, was green with vegetation and it lay just far"
"499"	"enough away to seem a Delectable Land, dreamy, reposeful, and inviting."
"500"	"Tom appeared on the sidewalk with a bucket of whitewash and a"
"501"	"long-handled brush. He surveyed the fence, and all gladness left him and"
"502"	"a deep melancholy settled down upon his spirit. Thirty yards of board"
"503"	"fence nine feet high. Life to him seemed hollow, and existence but a"
"504"	"burden. Sighing, he dipped his brush and passed it along the topmost"
"505"	"plank; repeated the operation; did it again; compared the insignificant"
"506"	"whitewashed streak with the far-reaching continent of unwhitewashed"
"507"	"fence, and sat down on a tree-box discouraged. Jim came skipping out at"
"508"	"the gate with a tin pail, and singing Buffalo Gals. Bringing water from"
"509"	"the town pump had always been hateful work in Tom’s eyes, before, but"
"510"	"now it did not strike him so. He remembered that there was company at"
"511"	"the pump. White, mulatto, and negro boys and girls were always there"
"512"	"waiting their turns, resting, trading playthings, quarrelling, fighting,"
"513"	"skylarking. And he remembered that although the pump was only a hundred"
"514"	"and fifty yards off, Jim never got back with a bucket of water under an"
"515"	"hour--and even then somebody generally had to go after him. Tom said:"
"516"	"“Say, Jim, I’ll fetch the water if you’ll whitewash some.”"
"517"	"Jim shook his head and said:"
"518"	"“Can’t, Mars Tom. Ole missis, she tole me I got to go an’ git dis water"
"519"	"an’ not stop foolin’ roun’ wid anybody. She say she spec’ Mars Tom gwine"
"520"	"to ax me to whitewash, an’ so she tole me go ‘long an’ ‘tend to my own"
"521"	"business--she ‘lowed _she’d_ ‘tend to de whitewashin’.”"
"522"	"“Oh, never you mind what she said, Jim. That’s the way she always talks."
"523"	"Gimme the bucket--I won’t be gone only a a minute. _She_ won’t ever"
"524"	"know.”"
"525"	"“Oh, I dasn’t, Mars Tom. Ole missis she’d take an’ tar de head off’n me."
"526"	"‘Deed she would.”"
"527"	"“_She_! She never licks anybody--whacks ‘em over the head with her"
"528"	"thimble--and who cares for that, I’d like to know. She talks awful, but"
"529"	"talk don’t hurt--anyways it don’t if she don’t cry. Jim, I’ll give you a"
"530"	"marvel. I’ll give you a white alley!”"
"531"	"Jim began to waver."
"532"	"“White alley, Jim! And it’s a bully taw.”"
"533"	"“My! Dat’s a mighty gay marvel, I tell you! But Mars Tom I’s powerful"
"534"	"‘fraid ole missis--”"
"535"	"“And besides, if you will I’ll show you my sore toe.”"
"536"	"Jim was only human--this attraction was too much for him. He put down"
"537"	"his pail, took the white alley, and bent over the toe with absorbing"
"538"	"interest while the bandage was being unwound. In another moment he"
"539"	"was flying down the street with his pail and a tingling rear, Tom was"
"540"	"whitewashing with vigor, and Aunt Polly was retiring from the field with"
"541"	"a slipper in her hand and triumph in her eye."
"542"	"But Tom’s energy did not last. He began to think of the fun he had"
"543"	"planned for this day, and his sorrows multiplied. Soon the free boys"
"544"	"would come tripping along on all sorts of delicious expeditions, and"
"545"	"they would make a world of fun of him for having to work--the very"
"546"	"thought of it burnt him like fire. He got out his worldly wealth and"
"547"	"examined it--bits of toys, marbles, and trash; enough to buy an exchange"
"548"	"of _work_, maybe, but not half enough to buy so much as half an hour"
"549"	"of pure freedom. So he returned his straitened means to his pocket, and"
"550"	"gave up the idea of trying to buy the boys. At this dark and hopeless"
"551"	"moment an inspiration burst upon him! Nothing less than a great,"
"552"	"magnificent inspiration."
"553"	"He took up his brush and went tranquilly to work. Ben Rogers hove in"
"554"	"sight presently--the very boy, of all boys, whose ridicule he had been"
"555"	"dreading. Ben’s gait was the hop-skip-and-jump--proof enough that his"
"556"	"heart was light and his anticipations high. He was eating an apple, and"
"557"	"giving a long, melodious whoop, at intervals, followed by a deep-toned"
"558"	"ding-dong-dong, ding-dong-dong, for he was personating a steamboat. As"
"559"	"he drew near, he slackened speed, took the middle of the street, leaned"
"560"	"far over to starboard and rounded to ponderously and with laborious pomp"
"561"	"and circumstance--for he was personating the Big Missouri, and considered"
"562"	"himself to be drawing nine feet of water. He was boat and captain and"
"563"	"engine-bells combined, so he had to imagine himself standing on his own"
"564"	"hurricane-deck giving the orders and executing them:"
"565"	"“Stop her, sir! Ting-a-ling-ling!” The headway ran almost out, and he"
"566"	"drew up slowly toward the sidewalk."
"567"	"“Ship up to back! Ting-a-ling-ling!” His arms straightened and stiffened"
"568"	"down his sides."
"569"	"“Set her back on the stabboard! Ting-a-ling-ling! Chow! ch-chow-wow!"
"570"	"Chow!” His right hand, mean-time, describing stately circles--for it was"
"571"	"representing a forty-foot wheel."
"572"	"“Let her go back on the labboard! Ting-a-ling-ling! Chow-ch-chow-chow!”"
"573"	" The left hand began to describe circles."
"574"	"“Stop the stabboard! Ting-a-ling-ling! Stop the labboard! Come ahead on"
"575"	"the stabboard! Stop her! Let your outside turn over slow! Ting-a-ling-ling!"
"576"	"Chow-ow-ow! Get out that head-line! _lively_ now! Come--out with"
"577"	"your spring-line--what’re you about there! Take a turn round that stump"
"578"	"with the bight of it! Stand by that stage, now--let her go! Done with"
"579"	"the engines, sir! Ting-a-ling-ling! SH’T! S’H’T! SH’T!” (trying the"
"580"	"gauge-cocks)."
"581"	"Tom went on whitewashing--paid no attention to the steamboat. Ben stared"
"582"	"a moment and then said: “_Hi-Yi! You’re_ up a stump, ain’t you!”"
"583"	"No answer. Tom surveyed his last touch with the eye of an artist, then"
"584"	"he gave his brush another gentle sweep and surveyed the result, as"
"585"	"before. Ben ranged up alongside of him. Tom’s mouth watered for the"
"586"	"apple, but he stuck to his work. Ben said:"
"587"	"“Hello, old chap, you got to work, hey?”"
"588"	"Tom wheeled suddenly and said:"
"589"	"“Why, it’s you, Ben! I warn’t noticing.”"
"590"	"“Say--I’m going in a-swimming, I am. Don’t you wish you could? But of"
"591"	"course you’d druther _work_--wouldn’t you? Course you would!”"
"592"	"Tom contemplated the boy a bit, and said:"
"593"	"“What do you call work?”"
"594"	"“Why, ain’t _that_ work?”"
"595"	"Tom resumed his whitewashing, and answered carelessly:"
"596"	"“Well, maybe it is, and maybe it ain’t. All I know, is, it suits Tom"
"597"	"Sawyer.”"
"598"	"“Oh come, now, you don’t mean to let on that you _like_ it?”"
"599"	"The brush continued to move."
"600"	"“Like it? Well, I don’t see why I oughtn’t to like it. Does a boy get a"
"601"	"chance to whitewash a fence every day?”"
"602"	"That put the thing in a new light. Ben stopped nibbling his apple."
"603"	"Tom swept his brush daintily back and forth--stepped back to note the"
"604"	"effect--added a touch here and there--criticised the effect again--Ben"
"605"	"watching every move and getting more and more interested, more and more"
"606"	"absorbed. Presently he said:"
"607"	"“Say, Tom, let _me_ whitewash a little.”"
"608"	"Tom considered, was about to consent; but he altered his mind:"
"609"	"“No--no--I reckon it wouldn’t hardly do, Ben. You see, Aunt Polly’s awful"
"610"	"particular about this fence--right here on the street, you know--but if it"
"611"	"was the back fence I wouldn’t mind and _she_ wouldn’t. Yes, she’s awful"
"612"	"particular about this fence; it’s got to be done very careful; I reckon"
"613"	"there ain’t one boy in a thousand, maybe two thousand, that can do it"
"614"	"the way it’s got to be done.”"
"615"	"“No--is that so? Oh come, now--lemme just try. Only just a little--I’d let"
"616"	"_you_, if you was me, Tom.”"
"617"	"“Ben, I’d like to, honest injun; but Aunt Polly--well, Jim wanted to do"
"618"	"it, but she wouldn’t let him; Sid wanted to do it, and she wouldn’t let"
"619"	"Sid. Now don’t you see how I’m fixed? If you was to tackle this fence"
"620"	"and anything was to happen to it--”"
"621"	"“Oh, shucks, I’ll be just as careful. Now lemme try. Say--I’ll give you"
"622"	"the core of my apple.”"
"623"	"“Well, here--No, Ben, now don’t. I’m afeard--”"
"624"	"“I’ll give you _all_ of it!”"
"625"	"Tom gave up the brush with reluctance in his face, but alacrity in his"
"626"	"heart. And while the late steamer Big Missouri worked and sweated in the"
"627"	"sun, the retired artist sat on a barrel in the shade close by,"
"628"	"dangled his legs, munched his apple, and planned the slaughter of more"
"629"	"innocents. There was no lack of material; boys happened along every"
"630"	"little while; they came to jeer, but remained to whitewash. By the time"
"631"	"Ben was fagged out, Tom had traded the next chance to Billy Fisher for"
"632"	"a kite, in good repair; and when he played out, Johnny Miller bought in"
"633"	"for a dead rat and a string to swing it with--and so on, and so on, hour"
"634"	"after hour. And when the middle of the afternoon came, from being a"
"635"	"poor poverty-stricken boy in the morning, Tom was literally rolling in"
"636"	"wealth. He had besides the things before mentioned, twelve marbles, part"
"637"	"of a jews-harp, a piece of blue bottle-glass to look through, a spool"
"638"	"cannon, a key that wouldn’t unlock anything, a fragment of chalk, a"
"639"	"glass stopper of a decanter, a tin soldier, a couple of tadpoles,"
"640"	"six fire-crackers, a kitten with only one eye, a brass door-knob, a"
"641"	"dog-collar--but no dog--the handle of a knife, four pieces of orange-peel,"
"642"	"and a dilapidated old window sash."
"643"	"He had had a nice, good, idle time all the while--plenty of company--and"
"644"	"the fence had three coats of whitewash on it! If he hadn’t run out of"
"645"	"whitewash he would have bankrupted every boy in the village."
"646"	"Tom said to himself that it was not such a hollow world, after all. He"
"647"	"had discovered a great law of human action, without knowing it--namely,"
"648"	"that in order to make a man or a boy covet a thing, it is only necessary"
"649"	"to make the thing difficult to attain. If he had been a great and"
"650"	"wise philosopher, like the writer of this book, he would now have"
"651"	"comprehended that Work consists of whatever a body is _obliged_ to do,"
"652"	"and that Play consists of whatever a body is not obliged to do. And"
"653"	"this would help him to understand why constructing artificial flowers or"
"654"	"performing on a tread-mill is work, while rolling ten-pins or climbing"
"655"	"Mont Blanc is only amusement. There are wealthy gentlemen in England"
"656"	"who drive four-horse passenger-coaches twenty or thirty miles on a"
"657"	"daily line, in the summer, because the privilege costs them considerable"
"658"	"money; but if they were offered wages for the service, that would turn"
"659"	"it into work and then they would resign."
"660"	"The boy mused awhile over the substantial change which had taken place"
"661"	"in his worldly circumstances, and then wended toward headquarters to"
"662"	"report."
"663"	"CHAPTER III"
"664"	"TOM presented himself before Aunt Polly, who was sitting by an"
"665"	"open window in a pleasant rearward apartment, which was bedroom,"
"666"	"breakfast-room, dining-room, and library, combined. The balmy summer"
"667"	"air, the restful quiet, the odor of the flowers, and the drowsing"
"668"	"murmur of the bees had had their effect, and she was nodding over her"
"669"	"knitting--for she had no company but the cat, and it was asleep in her"
"670"	"lap. Her spectacles were propped up on her gray head for safety. She had"
"671"	"thought that of course Tom had deserted long ago, and she wondered at"
"672"	"seeing him place himself in her power again in this intrepid way. He"
"673"	"said: “Mayn’t I go and play now, aunt?”"
"674"	"“What, a’ready? How much have you done?”"
"675"	"“It’s all done, aunt.”"
"676"	"“Tom, don’t lie to me--I can’t bear it.”"
"677"	"“I ain’t, aunt; it _is_ all done.”"
"678"	"Aunt Polly placed small trust in such evidence. She went out to see for"
"679"	"herself; and she would have been content to find twenty per cent. of"
"680"	"Tom’s statement true. When she found the entire fence white-washed, and"
"681"	"not only whitewashed but elaborately coated and recoated, and even a"
"682"	"streak added to the ground, her astonishment was almost unspeakable. She"
"683"	"said:"
"684"	"“Well, I never! There’s no getting round it, you can work when you’re a"
"685"	"mind to, Tom.” And then she diluted the compliment by adding, “But it’s"
"686"	"powerful seldom you’re a mind to, I’m bound to say. Well, go ‘long and"
"687"	"play; but mind you get back some time in a week, or I’ll tan you.”"
"688"	"She was so overcome by the splendor of his achievement that she took"
"689"	"him into the closet and selected a choice apple and delivered it to him,"
"690"	"along with an improving lecture upon the added value and flavor a treat"
"691"	"took to itself when it came without sin through virtuous effort."
"692"	"And while she closed with a happy Scriptural flourish, he “hooked” a"
"693"	"doughnut."
"694"	"Then he skipped out, and saw Sid just starting up the outside stairway"
"695"	"that led to the back rooms on the second floor. Clods were handy and"
"696"	"the air was full of them in a twinkling. They raged around Sid like a"
"697"	"hail-storm; and before Aunt Polly could collect her surprised faculties"
"698"	"and sally to the rescue, six or seven clods had taken personal effect,"
"699"	"and Tom was over the fence and gone. There was a gate, but as a general"
"700"	"thing he was too crowded for time to make use of it. His soul was at"
"701"	"peace, now that he had settled with Sid for calling attention to his"
"702"	"black thread and getting him into trouble."
"703"	"Tom skirted the block, and came round into a muddy alley that led by the"
"704"	"back of his aunt’s cow-stable. He presently got safely beyond the reach"
"705"	"of capture and punishment, and hastened toward the public square of the"
"706"	"village, where two “military” companies of boys had met for conflict,"
"707"	"according to previous appointment. Tom was General of one of these"
"708"	"armies, Joe Harper (a bosom friend) General of the other. These two"
"709"	"great commanders did not condescend to fight in person--that being better"
"710"	"suited to the still smaller fry--but sat together on an eminence"
"711"	"and conducted the field operations by orders delivered through"
"712"	"aides-de-camp. Tom’s army won a great victory, after a long and"
"713"	"hard-fought battle. Then the dead were counted, prisoners exchanged,"
"714"	"the terms of the next disagreement agreed upon, and the day for the"
"715"	"necessary battle appointed; after which the armies fell into line and"
"716"	"marched away, and Tom turned homeward alone."
"717"	"As he was passing by the house where Jeff Thatcher lived, he saw a new"
"718"	"girl in the garden--a lovely little blue-eyed creature with yellow"
"719"	"hair plaited into two long-tails, white summer frock and embroidered"
"720"	"pan-talettes. The fresh-crowned hero fell without firing a shot. A"
"721"	"certain Amy Lawrence vanished out of his heart and left not even a"
"722"	"memory of herself behind. He had thought he loved her to distraction;"
"723"	"he had regarded his passion as adoration; and behold it was only a poor"
"724"	"little evanescent partiality. He had been months winning her; she had"
"725"	"confessed hardly a week ago; he had been the happiest and the proudest"
"726"	"boy in the world only seven short days, and here in one instant of time"
"727"	"she had gone out of his heart like a casual stranger whose visit is"
"728"	"done."
"729"	"He worshipped this new angel with furtive eye, till he saw that she had"
"730"	"discovered him; then he pretended he did not know she was present, and"
"731"	"began to “show off” in all sorts of absurd boyish ways, in order to win"
"732"	"her admiration. He kept up this grotesque foolishness for some time;"
"733"	"but by-and-by, while he was in the midst of some dangerous gymnastic"
"734"	"performances, he glanced aside and saw that the little girl was wending"
"735"	"her way toward the house. Tom came up to the fence and leaned on it,"
"736"	"grieving, and hoping she would tarry yet awhile longer. She halted a"
"737"	"moment on the steps and then moved toward the door. Tom heaved a great"
"738"	"sigh as she put her foot on the threshold. But his face lit up,"
"739"	"right away, for she tossed a pansy over the fence a moment before she"
"740"	"disappeared."
"741"	"The boy ran around and stopped within a foot or two of the flower, and"
"742"	"then shaded his eyes with his hand and began to look down street as"
"743"	"if he had discovered something of interest going on in that direction."
"744"	"Presently he picked up a straw and began trying to balance it on his"
"745"	"nose, with his head tilted far back; and as he moved from side to side,"
"746"	"in his efforts, he edged nearer and nearer toward the pansy; finally his"
"747"	"bare foot rested upon it, his pliant toes closed upon it, and he hopped"
"748"	"away with the treasure and disappeared round the corner. But only for a"
"749"	"minute--only while he could button the flower inside his jacket, next"
"750"	"his heart--or next his stomach, possibly, for he was not much posted in"
"751"	"anatomy, and not hypercritical, anyway."
"752"	"He returned, now, and hung about the fence till nightfall, “showing"
"753"	"off,” as before; but the girl never exhibited herself again, though Tom"
"754"	"comforted himself a little with the hope that she had been near some"
"755"	"window, meantime, and been aware of his attentions. Finally he strode"
"756"	"home reluctantly, with his poor head full of visions."
"757"	"All through supper his spirits were so high that his aunt wondered “what"
"758"	"had got into the child.” He took a good scolding about clodding Sid, and"
"759"	"did not seem to mind it in the least. He tried to steal sugar under his"
"760"	"aunt’s very nose, and got his knuckles rapped for it. He said:"
"761"	"“Aunt, you don’t whack Sid when he takes it.”"
"762"	"“Well, Sid don’t torment a body the way you do. You’d be always into"
"763"	"that sugar if I warn’t watching you.”"
"764"	"Presently she stepped into the kitchen, and Sid, happy in his immunity,"
"765"	"reached for the sugar-bowl--a sort of glorying over Tom which was"
"766"	"wellnigh unbearable. But Sid’s fingers slipped and the bowl dropped and"
"767"	"broke. Tom was in ecstasies. In such ecstasies that he even controlled"
"768"	"his tongue and was silent. He said to himself that he would not speak a"
"769"	"word, even when his aunt came in, but would sit perfectly still till she"
"770"	"asked who did the mischief; and then he would tell, and there would be"
"771"	"nothing so good in the world as to see that pet model “catch it.” He was"
"772"	"so brimful of exultation that he could hardly hold himself when the old"
"773"	"lady came back and stood above the wreck discharging lightnings of wrath"
"774"	"from over her spectacles. He said to himself, “Now it’s coming!” And the"
"775"	"next instant he was sprawling on the floor! The potent palm was uplifted"
"776"	"to strike again when Tom cried out:"
"777"	"“Hold on, now, what ‘er you belting _me_ for?--Sid broke it!”"
"778"	"Aunt Polly paused, perplexed, and Tom looked for healing pity. But when"
"779"	"she got her tongue again, she only said:"
"780"	"“Umf! Well, you didn’t get a lick amiss, I reckon. You been into some"
"781"	"other audacious mischief when I wasn’t around, like enough.”"
"782"	"Then her conscience reproached her, and she yearned to say something"
"783"	"kind and loving; but she judged that this would be construed into a"
"784"	"confession that she had been in the wrong, and discipline forbade that."
"785"	"So she kept silence, and went about her affairs with a troubled heart."
"786"	"Tom sulked in a corner and exalted his woes. He knew that in her heart"
"787"	"his aunt was on her knees to him, and he was morosely gratified by the"
"788"	"consciousness of it. He would hang out no signals, he would take notice"
"789"	"of none. He knew that a yearning glance fell upon him, now and then,"
"790"	"through a film of tears, but he refused recognition of it. He pictured"
"791"	"himself lying sick unto death and his aunt bending over him beseeching"
"792"	"one little forgiving word, but he would turn his face to the wall, and"
"793"	"die with that word unsaid. Ah, how would she feel then? And he pictured"
"794"	"himself brought home from the river, dead, with his curls all wet, and"
"795"	"his sore heart at rest. How she would throw herself upon him, and how"
"796"	"her tears would fall like rain, and her lips pray God to give her back"
"797"	"her boy and she would never, never abuse him any more! But he would"
"798"	"lie there cold and white and make no sign--a poor little sufferer, whose"
"799"	"griefs were at an end. He so worked upon his feelings with the pathos of"
"800"	"these dreams, that he had to keep swallowing, he was so like to choke;"
"801"	"and his eyes swam in a blur of water, which overflowed when he winked,"
"802"	"and ran down and trickled from the end of his nose. And such a luxury to"
"803"	"him was this petting of his sorrows, that he could not bear to have any"
"804"	"worldly cheeriness or any grating delight intrude upon it; it was too"
"805"	"sacred for such contact; and so, presently, when his cousin Mary danced"
"806"	"in, all alive with the joy of seeing home again after an age-long visit"
"807"	"of one week to the country, he got up and moved in clouds and darkness"
"808"	"out at one door as she brought song and sunshine in at the other."
"809"	"He wandered far from the accustomed haunts of boys, and sought desolate"
"810"	"places that were in harmony with his spirit. A log raft in the river"
"811"	"invited him, and he seated himself on its outer edge and contemplated"
"812"	"the dreary vastness of the stream, wishing, the while, that he could"
"813"	"only be drowned, all at once and unconsciously, without undergoing the"
"814"	"uncomfortable routine devised by nature. Then he thought of his flower."
"815"	"He got it out, rumpled and wilted, and it mightily increased his dismal"
"816"	"felicity. He wondered if she would pity him if she knew? Would she"
"817"	"cry, and wish that she had a right to put her arms around his neck and"
"818"	"comfort him? Or would she turn coldly away like all the hollow world?"
"819"	"This picture brought such an agony of pleasurable suffering that he"
"820"	"worked it over and over again in his mind and set it up in new and"
"821"	"varied lights, till he wore it threadbare. At last he rose up sighing"
"822"	"and departed in the darkness."
"823"	"About half-past nine or ten o’clock he came along the deserted street to"
"824"	"where the Adored Unknown lived; he paused a moment; no sound fell upon"
"825"	"his listening ear; a candle was casting a dull glow upon the curtain"
"826"	"of a second-story window. Was the sacred presence there? He climbed the"
"827"	"fence, threaded his stealthy way through the plants, till he stood under"
"828"	"that window; he looked up at it long, and with emotion; then he laid him"
"829"	"down on the ground under it, disposing himself upon his back, with his"
"830"	"hands clasped upon his breast and holding his poor wilted flower."
"831"	"And thus he would die--out in the cold world, with no shelter over his"
"832"	"homeless head, no friendly hand to wipe the death-damps from his brow,"
"833"	"no loving face to bend pityingly over him when the great agony came. And"
"834"	"thus _she_ would see him when she looked out upon the glad morning, and"
"835"	"oh! would she drop one little tear upon his poor, lifeless form, would"
"836"	"she heave one little sigh to see a bright young life so rudely blighted,"
"837"	"so untimely cut down?"
"838"	"The window went up, a maid-servant’s discordant voice profaned the holy"
"839"	"calm, and a deluge of water drenched the prone martyr’s remains!"
"840"	"The strangling hero sprang up with a relieving snort. There was a whiz"
"841"	"as of a missile in the air, mingled with the murmur of a curse, a sound"
"842"	"as of shivering glass followed, and a small, vague form went over the"
"843"	"fence and shot away in the gloom."
"844"	"Not long after, as Tom, all undressed for bed, was surveying his"
"845"	"drenched garments by the light of a tallow dip, Sid woke up; but if he"
"846"	"had any dim idea of making any “references to allusions,” he thought"
"847"	"better of it and held his peace, for there was danger in Tom’s eye."
"848"	"Tom turned in without the added vexation of prayers, and Sid made mental"
"849"	"note of the omission."
"850"	"CHAPTER IV"
"851"	"THE sun rose upon a tranquil world, and beamed down upon the peaceful"
"852"	"village like a benediction. Breakfast over, Aunt Polly had family"
"853"	"worship: it began with a prayer built from the ground up of solid"
"854"	"courses of Scriptural quotations, welded together with a thin mortar of"
"855"	"originality; and from the summit of this she delivered a grim chapter of"
"856"	"the Mosaic Law, as from Sinai."
"857"	"Then Tom girded up his loins, so to speak, and went to work to “get"
"858"	"his verses.” Sid had learned his lesson days before. Tom bent all his"
"859"	"energies to the memorizing of five verses, and he chose part of the"
"860"	"Sermon on the Mount, because he could find no verses that were shorter."
"861"	"At the end of half an hour Tom had a vague general idea of his lesson,"
"862"	"but no more, for his mind was traversing the whole field of human"
"863"	"thought, and his hands were busy with distracting recreations. Mary took"
"864"	"his book to hear him recite, and he tried to find his way through the"
"865"	"fog:"
"866"	"“Blessed are the--a--a--”"
"867"	"“Poor”--"
"868"	"“Yes--poor; blessed are the poor--a--a--”"
"869"	"“In spirit--”"
"870"	"“In spirit; blessed are the poor in spirit, for they--they--”"
"871"	"“_Theirs_--”"
"872"	"“For _theirs_. Blessed are the poor in spirit, for theirs is the kingdom"
"873"	"of heaven. Blessed are they that mourn, for they--they--”"
"874"	"“Sh--”"
"875"	"“For they--a--”"
"876"	"“S, H, A--”"
"877"	"“For they S, H--Oh, I don’t know what it is!”"
"878"	"“_Shall_!”"
"879"	"“Oh, _shall_! for they shall--for they shall--a--a--shall mourn--a--a--blessed"
"880"	"are they that shall--they that--a--they that shall mourn, for they"
"881"	"shall--a--shall _what_? Why don’t you tell me, Mary?--what do you want to"
"882"	"be so mean for?”"
"883"	"“Oh, Tom, you poor thick-headed thing, I’m not teasing you. I wouldn’t"
"884"	"do that. You must go and learn it again. Don’t you be discouraged, Tom,"
"885"	"you’ll manage it--and if you do, I’ll give you something ever so nice."
"886"	"There, now, that’s a good boy.”"
"887"	"“All right! What is it, Mary, tell me what it is.”"
"888"	"“Never you mind, Tom. You know if I say it’s nice, it is nice.”"
"889"	"“You bet you that’s so, Mary. All right, I’ll tackle it again.”"
"890"	"And he did “tackle it again”--and under the double pressure of curiosity"
"891"	"and prospective gain he did it with such spirit that he accomplished a"
"892"	"shining success. Mary gave him a brand-new “Barlow” knife worth twelve"
"893"	"and a half cents; and the convulsion of delight that swept his system"
"894"	"shook him to his foundations. True, the knife would not cut anything,"
"895"	"but it was a “sure-enough” Barlow, and there was inconceivable grandeur"
"896"	"in that--though where the Western boys ever got the idea that such a"
"897"	"weapon could possibly be counterfeited to its injury is an imposing"
"898"	"mystery and will always remain so, perhaps. Tom contrived to scarify the"
"899"	"cupboard with it, and was arranging to begin on the bureau, when he was"
"900"	"called off to dress for Sunday-school."
"901"	"Mary gave him a tin basin of water and a piece of soap, and he went"
"902"	"outside the door and set the basin on a little bench there; then he"
"903"	"dipped the soap in the water and laid it down; turned up his sleeves;"
"904"	"poured out the water on the ground, gently, and then entered the kitchen"
"905"	"and began to wipe his face diligently on the towel behind the door. But"
"906"	"Mary removed the towel and said:"
"907"	"“Now ain’t you ashamed, Tom. You mustn’t be so bad. Water won’t hurt"
"908"	"you.”"
"909"	"Tom was a trifle disconcerted. The basin was refilled, and this time he"
"910"	"stood over it a little while, gathering resolution; took in a big breath"
"911"	"and began. When he entered the kitchen presently, with both eyes shut"
"912"	"and groping for the towel with his hands, an honorable testimony of"
"913"	"suds and water was dripping from his face. But when he emerged from"
"914"	"the towel, he was not yet satisfactory, for the clean territory stopped"
"915"	"short at his chin and his jaws, like a mask; below and beyond this line"
"916"	"there was a dark expanse of unirrigated soil that spread downward in"
"917"	"front and backward around his neck. Mary took him in hand, and when she"
"918"	"was done with him he was a man and a brother, without distinction of"
"919"	"color, and his saturated hair was neatly brushed, and its short curls"
"920"	"wrought into a dainty and symmetrical general effect. [He privately"
"921"	"smoothed out the curls, with labor and difficulty, and plastered his"
"922"	"hair close down to his head; for he held curls to be effeminate, and his"
"923"	"own filled his life with bitterness.] Then Mary got out a suit of his"
"924"	"clothing that had been used only on Sundays during two years--they were"
"925"	"simply called his “other clothes”--and so by that we know the size of his"
"926"	"wardrobe. The girl “put him to rights” after he had dressed himself;"
"927"	"she buttoned his neat roundabout up to his chin, turned his vast shirt"
"928"	"collar down over his shoulders, brushed him off and crowned him with"
"929"	"his speckled straw hat. He now looked exceedingly improved and"
"930"	"uncomfortable. He was fully as uncomfortable as he looked; for there"
"931"	"was a restraint about whole clothes and cleanliness that galled him. He"
"932"	"hoped that Mary would forget his shoes, but the hope was blighted; she"
"933"	"coated them thoroughly with tallow, as was the custom, and brought"
"934"	"them out. He lost his temper and said he was always being made to do"
"935"	"everything he didn’t want to do. But Mary said, persuasively:"
"936"	"“Please, Tom--that’s a good boy.”"
"937"	"So he got into the shoes snarling. Mary was soon ready, and the three"
"938"	"children set out for Sunday-school--a place that Tom hated with his whole"
"939"	"heart; but Sid and Mary were fond of it."
"940"	"Sabbath-school hours were from nine to half-past ten; and then church"
"941"	"service. Two of the children always remained for the sermon voluntarily,"
"942"	"and the other always remained too--for stronger reasons. The church’s"
"943"	"high-backed, uncushioned pews would seat about three hundred persons;"
"944"	"the edifice was but a small, plain affair, with a sort of pine board"
"945"	"tree-box on top of it for a steeple. At the door Tom dropped back a step"
"946"	"and accosted a Sunday-dressed comrade:"
"947"	"“Say, Billy, got a yaller ticket?”"
"948"	"“Yes.”"
"949"	"“What’ll you take for her?”"
"950"	"“What’ll you give?”"
"951"	"“Piece of lickrish and a fish-hook.”"
"952"	"“Less see ‘em.”"
"953"	"Tom exhibited. They were satisfactory, and the property changed hands."
"954"	"Then Tom traded a couple of white alleys for three red tickets, and some"
"955"	"small trifle or other for a couple of blue ones. He waylaid other"
"956"	"boys as they came, and went on buying tickets of various colors ten"
"957"	"or fifteen minutes longer. He entered the church, now, with a swarm"
"958"	"of clean and noisy boys and girls, proceeded to his seat and started"
"959"	"a quarrel with the first boy that came handy. The teacher, a grave,"
"960"	"elderly man, interfered; then turned his back a moment and Tom pulled a"
"961"	"boy’s hair in the next bench, and was absorbed in his book when the boy"
"962"	"turned around; stuck a pin in another boy, presently, in order to hear"
"963"	"him say “Ouch!” and got a new reprimand from his teacher. Tom’s whole"
"964"	"class were of a pattern--restless, noisy, and troublesome. When they came"
"965"	"to recite their lessons, not one of them knew his verses perfectly, but"
"966"	"had to be prompted all along. However, they worried through, and each"
"967"	"got his reward--in small blue tickets, each with a passage of Scripture"
"968"	"on it; each blue ticket was pay for two verses of the recitation. Ten"
"969"	"blue tickets equalled a red one, and could be exchanged for it; ten red"
"970"	"tickets equalled a yellow one; for ten yellow tickets the superintendent"
"971"	"gave a very plainly bound Bible (worth forty cents in those easy"
"972"	"times) to the pupil. How many of my readers would have the industry and"
"973"	"application to memorize two thousand verses, even for a Dore Bible? And"
"974"	"yet Mary had acquired two Bibles in this way--it was the patient work of"
"975"	"two years--and a boy of German parentage had won four or five. He once"
"976"	"recited three thousand verses without stopping; but the strain upon his"
"977"	"mental faculties was too great, and he was little better than an idiot"
"978"	"from that day forth--a grievous misfortune for the school, for on great"
"979"	"occasions, before company, the superintendent (as Tom expressed it)"
"980"	"had always made this boy come out and “spread himself.” Only the older"
"981"	"pupils managed to keep their tickets and stick to their tedious work"
"982"	"long enough to get a Bible, and so the delivery of one of these prizes"
"983"	"was a rare and noteworthy circumstance; the successful pupil was so"
"984"	"great and conspicuous for that day that on the spot every scholar’s"
"985"	"heart was fired with a fresh ambition that often lasted a couple"
"986"	"of weeks. It is possible that Tom’s mental stomach had never really"
"987"	"hungered for one of those prizes, but unquestionably his entire being"
"988"	"had for many a day longed for the glory and the eclat that came with it."
"989"	"In due course the superintendent stood up in front of the pulpit, with"
"990"	"a closed hymn-book in his hand and his forefinger inserted between its"
"991"	"leaves, and commanded attention. When a Sunday-school superintendent"
"992"	"makes his customary little speech, a hymn-book in the hand is as"
"993"	"necessary as is the inevitable sheet of music in the hand of a singer"
"994"	"who stands forward on the platform and sings a solo at a concert--though"
"995"	"why, is a mystery: for neither the hymn-book nor the sheet of music"
"996"	"is ever referred to by the sufferer. This superintendent was a slim"
"997"	"creature of thirty-five, with a sandy goatee and short sandy hair; he"
"998"	"wore a stiff standing-collar whose upper edge almost reached his ears"
"999"	"and whose sharp points curved forward abreast the corners of his mouth--a"
"1000"	"fence that compelled a straight lookout ahead, and a turning of the"
"1001"	"whole body when a side view was required; his chin was propped on a"
"1002"	"spreading cravat which was as broad and as long as a bank-note, and had"
"1003"	"fringed ends; his boot toes were turned sharply up, in the fashion"
"1004"	"of the day, like sleigh-runners--an effect patiently and laboriously"
"1005"	"produced by the young men by sitting with their toes pressed against a"
"1006"	"wall for hours together. Mr. Walters was very earnest of mien, and very"
"1007"	"sincere and honest at heart; and he held sacred things and places"
"1008"	"in such reverence, and so separated them from worldly matters, that"
"1009"	"unconsciously to himself his Sunday-school voice had acquired a peculiar"
"1010"	"intonation which was wholly absent on week-days. He began after this"
"1011"	"fashion:"
"1012"	"“Now, children, I want you all to sit up just as straight and pretty as"
"1013"	"you can and give me all your attention for a minute or two. There--that"
"1014"	"is it. That is the way good little boys and girls should do. I see one"
"1015"	"little girl who is looking out of the window--I am afraid she thinks I"
"1016"	"am out there somewhere--perhaps up in one of the trees making a speech"
"1017"	"to the little birds. [Applausive titter.] I want to tell you how good it"
"1018"	"makes me feel to see so many bright, clean little faces assembled in a"
"1019"	"place like this, learning to do right and be good.” And so forth and so"
"1020"	"on. It is not necessary to set down the rest of the oration. It was of a"
"1021"	"pattern which does not vary, and so it is familiar to us all."
"1022"	"The latter third of the speech was marred by the resumption of fights"
"1023"	"and other recreations among certain of the bad boys, and by fidgetings"
"1024"	"and whisperings that extended far and wide, washing even to the bases of"
"1025"	"isolated and incorruptible rocks like Sid and Mary. But now every sound"
"1026"	"ceased suddenly, with the subsidence of Mr. Walters’ voice, and the"
"1027"	"conclusion of the speech was received with a burst of silent gratitude."
"1028"	"A good part of the whispering had been occasioned by an event which was"
"1029"	"more or less rare--the entrance of visitors: lawyer Thatcher, accompanied"
"1030"	"by a very feeble and aged man; a fine, portly, middle-aged gentleman"
"1031"	"with iron-gray hair; and a dignified lady who was doubtless the latter’s"
"1032"	"wife. The lady was leading a child. Tom had been restless and full of"
"1033"	"chafings and repinings; conscience-smitten, too--he could not meet Amy"
"1034"	"Lawrence’s eye, he could not brook her loving gaze. But when he saw this"
"1035"	"small newcomer his soul was all ablaze with bliss in a moment. The next"
"1036"	"moment he was “showing off” with all his might--cuffing boys, pulling"
"1037"	"hair, making faces--in a word, using every art that seemed likely to"
"1038"	"fascinate a girl and win her applause. His exaltation had but one"
"1039"	"alloy--the memory of his humiliation in this angel’s garden--and that"
"1040"	"record in sand was fast washing out, under the waves of happiness that"
"1041"	"were sweeping over it now."
"1042"	"The visitors were given the highest seat of honor, and as soon as Mr."
"1043"	"Walters’ speech was finished, he introduced them to the school. The"
"1044"	"middle-aged man turned out to be a prodigious personage--no less a one"
"1045"	"than the county judge--altogether the most august creation these children"
"1046"	"had ever looked upon--and they wondered what kind of material he was made"
"1047"	"of--and they half wanted to hear him roar, and were half afraid he might,"
"1048"	"too. He was from Constantinople, twelve miles away--so he had travelled,"
"1049"	"and seen the world--these very eyes had looked upon the county"
"1050"	"court-house--which was said to have a tin roof. The awe which these"
"1051"	"reflections inspired was attested by the impressive silence and the"
"1052"	"ranks of staring eyes. This was the great Judge Thatcher, brother of"
"1053"	"their own lawyer. Jeff Thatcher immediately went forward, to be familiar"
"1054"	"with the great man and be envied by the school. It would have been music"
"1055"	"to his soul to hear the whisperings:"
"1056"	"“Look at him, Jim! He’s a going up there. Say--look! he’s a going to"
"1057"	"shake hands with him--he _is_ shaking hands with him! By jings, don’t you"
"1058"	"wish you was Jeff?”"
"1059"	"Mr. Walters fell to “showing off,” with all sorts of official bustlings"
"1060"	"and activities, giving orders, delivering judgments, discharging"
"1061"	"directions here, there, everywhere that he could find a target. The"
"1062"	"librarian “showed off”--running hither and thither with his arms full of"
"1063"	"books and making a deal of the splutter and fuss that insect authority"
"1064"	"delights in. The young lady teachers “showed off”--bending sweetly over"
"1065"	"pupils that were lately being boxed, lifting pretty warning fingers"
"1066"	"at bad little boys and patting good ones lovingly. The young gentlemen"
"1067"	"teachers “showed off” with small scoldings and other little displays of"
"1068"	"authority and fine attention to discipline--and most of the teachers, of"
"1069"	"both sexes, found business up at the library, by the pulpit; and it was"
"1070"	"business that frequently had to be done over again two or three times"
"1071"	"(with much seeming vexation). The little girls “showed off” in various"
"1072"	"ways, and the little boys “showed off” with such diligence that the air"
"1073"	"was thick with paper wads and the murmur of scufflings. And above it"
"1074"	"all the great man sat and beamed a majestic judicial smile upon all"
"1075"	"the house, and warmed himself in the sun of his own grandeur--for he was"
"1076"	"“showing off,” too."
"1077"	"There was only one thing wanting to make Mr. Walters’ ecstasy complete,"
"1078"	"and that was a chance to deliver a Bible-prize and exhibit a prodigy."
"1079"	"Several pupils had a few yellow tickets, but none had enough--he had been"
"1080"	"around among the star pupils inquiring. He would have given worlds, now,"
"1081"	"to have that German lad back again with a sound mind."
"1082"	"And now at this moment, when hope was dead, Tom Sawyer came forward with"
"1083"	"nine yellow tickets, nine red tickets, and ten blue ones, and demanded"
"1084"	"a Bible. This was a thunderbolt out of a clear sky. Walters was not"
"1085"	"expecting an application from this source for the next ten years. But"
"1086"	"there was no getting around it--here were the certified checks, and they"
"1087"	"were good for their face. Tom was therefore elevated to a place with"
"1088"	"the Judge and the other elect, and the great news was announced from"
"1089"	"headquarters. It was the most stunning surprise of the decade, and"
"1090"	"so profound was the sensation that it lifted the new hero up to the"
"1091"	"judicial one’s altitude, and the school had two marvels to gaze upon"
"1092"	"in place of one. The boys were all eaten up with envy--but those that"
"1093"	"suffered the bitterest pangs were those who perceived too late that they"
"1094"	"themselves had contributed to this hated splendor by trading tickets to"
"1095"	"Tom for the wealth he had amassed in selling whitewashing privileges."
"1096"	"These despised themselves, as being the dupes of a wily fraud, a"
"1097"	"guileful snake in the grass."
"1098"	"The prize was delivered to Tom with as much effusion as the"
"1099"	"superintendent could pump up under the circumstances; but it lacked"
"1100"	"somewhat of the true gush, for the poor fellow’s instinct taught him"
"1101"	"that there was a mystery here that could not well bear the light,"
"1102"	"perhaps; it was simply preposterous that this boy had warehoused two"
"1103"	"thousand sheaves of Scriptural wisdom on his premises--a dozen would"
"1104"	"strain his capacity, without a doubt."
"1105"	"Amy Lawrence was proud and glad, and she tried to make Tom see it in"
"1106"	"her face--but he wouldn’t look. She wondered; then she was just a grain"
"1107"	"troubled; next a dim suspicion came and went--came again; she watched;"
"1108"	"a furtive glance told her worlds--and then her heart broke, and she was"
"1109"	"jealous, and angry, and the tears came and she hated everybody. Tom most"
"1110"	"of all (she thought)."
"1111"	"Tom was introduced to the Judge; but his tongue was tied, his breath"
"1112"	"would hardly come, his heart quaked--partly because of the awful"
"1113"	"greatness of the man, but mainly because he was her parent. He would"
"1114"	"have liked to fall down and worship him, if it were in the dark. The"
"1115"	"Judge put his hand on Tom’s head and called him a fine little man, and"
"1116"	"asked him what his name was. The boy stammered, gasped, and got it out:"
"1117"	"“Tom.”"
"1118"	"“Oh, no, not Tom--it is--”"
"1119"	"“Thomas.”"
"1120"	"“Ah, that’s it. I thought there was more to it, maybe. That’s very well."
"1121"	"But you’ve another one I daresay, and you’ll tell it to me, won’t you?”"
"1122"	"“Tell the gentleman your other name, Thomas,” said Walters, “and say"
"1123"	"sir. You mustn’t forget your manners.”"
"1124"	"“Thomas Sawyer--sir.”"
"1125"	"“That’s it! That’s a good boy. Fine boy. Fine, manly little fellow. Two"
"1126"	"thousand verses is a great many--very, very great many. And you never can"
"1127"	"be sorry for the trouble you took to learn them; for knowledge is worth"
"1128"	"more than anything there is in the world; it’s what makes great men"
"1129"	"and good men; you’ll be a great man and a good man yourself, some"
"1130"	"day, Thomas, and then you’ll look back and say, It’s all owing to the"
"1131"	"precious Sunday-school privileges of my boyhood--it’s all owing to"
"1132"	"my dear teachers that taught me to learn--it’s all owing to the good"
"1133"	"superintendent, who encouraged me, and watched over me, and gave me a"
"1134"	"beautiful Bible--a splendid elegant Bible--to keep and have it all for my"
"1135"	"own, always--it’s all owing to right bringing up! That is what you will"
"1136"	"say, Thomas--and you wouldn’t take any money for those two thousand"
"1137"	"verses--no indeed you wouldn’t. And now you wouldn’t mind telling me and"
"1138"	"this lady some of the things you’ve learned--no, I know you wouldn’t--for"
"1139"	"we are proud of little boys that learn. Now, no doubt you know the names"
"1140"	"of all the twelve disciples. Won’t you tell us the names of the first"
"1141"	"two that were appointed?”"
"1142"	"Tom was tugging at a button-hole and looking sheepish. He blushed,"
"1143"	"now, and his eyes fell. Mr. Walters’ heart sank within him. He said"
"1144"	"to himself, it is not possible that the boy can answer the simplest"
"1145"	"question--why _did_ the Judge ask him? Yet he felt obliged to speak up"
"1146"	"and say:"
"1147"	"“Answer the gentleman, Thomas--don’t be afraid.”"
"1148"	"Tom still hung fire."
"1149"	"“Now I know you’ll tell me,” said the lady. “The names of the first two"
"1150"	"disciples were--”"
"1151"	"“_David And Goliah!_”"
"1152"	"Let us draw the curtain of charity over the rest of the scene."
"1153"	"CHAPTER V"
"1154"	"ABOUT half-past ten the cracked bell of the small church began to ring,"
"1155"	"and presently the people began to gather for the morning sermon. The"
"1156"	"Sunday-school children distributed themselves about the house and"
"1157"	"occupied pews with their parents, so as to be under supervision. Aunt"
"1158"	"Polly came, and Tom and Sid and Mary sat with her--Tom being placed next"
"1159"	"the aisle, in order that he might be as far away from the open window"
"1160"	"and the seductive outside summer scenes as possible. The crowd filed up"
"1161"	"the aisles: the aged and needy postmaster, who had seen better days;"
"1162"	"the mayor and his wife--for they had a mayor there, among other"
"1163"	"unnecessaries; the justice of the peace; the widow Douglass, fair,"
"1164"	"smart, and forty, a generous, good-hearted soul and well-to-do, her hill"
"1165"	"mansion the only palace in the town, and the most hospitable and much"
"1166"	"the most lavish in the matter of festivities that St. Petersburg could"
"1167"	"boast; the bent and venerable Major and Mrs. Ward; lawyer Riverson, the"
"1168"	"new notable from a distance; next the belle of the village, followed by"
"1169"	"a troop of lawn-clad and ribbon-decked young heart-breakers; then all"
"1170"	"the young clerks in town in a body--for they had stood in the vestibule"
"1171"	"sucking their cane-heads, a circling wall of oiled and simpering"
"1172"	"admirers, till the last girl had run their gantlet; and last of all came"
"1173"	"the Model Boy, Willie Mufferson, taking as heedful care of his mother as"
"1174"	"if she were cut glass. He always brought his mother to church, and was"
"1175"	"the pride of all the matrons. The boys all hated him, he was so"
"1176"	"good. And besides, he had been “thrown up to them” so much. His"
"1177"	"white handkerchief was hanging out of his pocket behind, as usual on"
"1178"	"Sundays--accidentally. Tom had no handkerchief, and he looked upon boys"
"1179"	"who had as snobs."
"1180"	"The congregation being fully assembled, now, the bell rang once more,"
"1181"	"to warn laggards and stragglers, and then a solemn hush fell upon the"
"1182"	"church which was only broken by the tittering and whispering of the"
"1183"	"choir in the gallery. The choir always tittered and whispered all"
"1184"	"through service. There was once a church choir that was not ill-bred,"
"1185"	"but I have forgotten where it was, now. It was a great many years ago,"
"1186"	"and I can scarcely remember anything about it, but I think it was in"
"1187"	"some foreign country."
"1188"	"The minister gave out the hymn, and read it through with a relish, in a"
"1189"	"peculiar style which was much admired in that part of the country. His"
"1190"	"voice began on a medium key and climbed steadily up till it reached a"
"1191"	"certain point, where it bore with strong emphasis upon the topmost word"
"1192"	"and then plunged down as if from a spring-board:"
"1193"	"Shall I be car-ri-ed toe the skies, on flow’ry _beds_ of ease,"
"1194"	"Whilst others fight to win the prize, and sail thro’ _blood_-y seas?"
"1195"	"He was regarded as a wonderful reader. At church “sociables” he was"
"1196"	"always called upon to read poetry; and when he was through, the ladies"
"1197"	"would lift up their hands and let them fall helplessly in their laps,"
"1198"	"and “wall” their eyes, and shake their heads, as much as to say, “Words"
"1199"	"cannot express it; it is too beautiful, TOO beautiful for this mortal"
"1200"	"earth.”"
"1201"	"After the hymn had been sung, the Rev. Mr. Sprague turned himself into"
"1202"	"a bulletin-board, and read off “notices” of meetings and societies and"
"1203"	"things till it seemed that the list would stretch out to the crack of"
"1204"	"doom--a queer custom which is still kept up in America, even in cities,"
"1205"	"away here in this age of abundant newspapers. Often, the less there is"
"1206"	"to justify a traditional custom, the harder it is to get rid of it."
"1207"	"And now the minister prayed. A good, generous prayer it was, and went"
"1208"	"into details: it pleaded for the church, and the little children of the"
"1209"	"church; for the other churches of the village; for the village itself;"
"1210"	"for the county; for the State; for the State officers; for the United"
"1211"	"States; for the churches of the United States; for Congress; for the"
"1212"	"President; for the officers of the Government; for poor sailors, tossed"
"1213"	"by stormy seas; for the oppressed millions groaning under the heel of"
"1214"	"European monarchies and Oriental despotisms; for such as have the light"
"1215"	"and the good tidings, and yet have not eyes to see nor ears to hear"
"1216"	"withal; for the heathen in the far islands of the sea; and closed with"
"1217"	"a supplication that the words he was about to speak might find grace"
"1218"	"and favor, and be as seed sown in fertile ground, yielding in time a"
"1219"	"grateful harvest of good. Amen."
"1220"	"There was a rustling of dresses, and the standing congregation sat down."
"1221"	"The boy whose history this book relates did not enjoy the prayer, he"
"1222"	"only endured it--if he even did that much. He was restive all through it;"
"1223"	"he kept tally of the details of the prayer, unconsciously--for he was not"
"1224"	"listening, but he knew the ground of old, and the clergyman’s regular"
"1225"	"route over it--and when a little trifle of new matter was interlarded,"
"1226"	"his ear detected it and his whole nature resented it; he considered"
"1227"	"additions unfair, and scoundrelly. In the midst of the prayer a fly had"
"1228"	"lit on the back of the pew in front of him and tortured his spirit by"
"1229"	"calmly rubbing its hands together, embracing its head with its arms, and"
"1230"	"polishing it so vigorously that it seemed to almost part company with"
"1231"	"the body, and the slender thread of a neck was exposed to view; scraping"
"1232"	"its wings with its hind legs and smoothing them to its body as if they"
"1233"	"had been coat-tails; going through its whole toilet as tranquilly as if"
"1234"	"it knew it was perfectly safe. As indeed it was; for as sorely as Tom’s"
"1235"	"hands itched to grab for it they did not dare--he believed his soul would"
"1236"	"be instantly destroyed if he did such a thing while the prayer was going"
"1237"	"on. But with the closing sentence his hand began to curve and steal"
"1238"	"forward; and the instant the “Amen” was out the fly was a prisoner of"
"1239"	"war. His aunt detected the act and made him let it go."
"1240"	"The minister gave out his text and droned along monotonously through an"
"1241"	"argument that was so prosy that many a head by and by began to nod--and"
"1242"	"yet it was an argument that dealt in limitless fire and brimstone and"
"1243"	"thinned the predestined elect down to a company so small as to be hardly"
"1244"	"worth the saving. Tom counted the pages of the sermon; after church he"
"1245"	"always knew how many pages there had been, but he seldom knew anything"
"1246"	"else about the discourse. However, this time he was really interested"
"1247"	"for a little while. The minister made a grand and moving picture of the"
"1248"	"assembling together of the world’s hosts at the millennium when the lion"
"1249"	"and the lamb should lie down together and a little child should lead"
"1250"	"them. But the pathos, the lesson, the moral of the great spectacle"
"1251"	"were lost upon the boy; he only thought of the conspicuousness of the"
"1252"	"principal character before the on-looking nations; his face lit with the"
"1253"	"thought, and he said to himself that he wished he could be that child,"
"1254"	"if it was a tame lion."
"1255"	"Now he lapsed into suffering again, as the dry argument was resumed."
"1256"	"Presently he bethought him of a treasure he had and got it out. It was"
"1257"	"a large black beetle with formidable jaws--a “pinchbug,” he called it. It"
"1258"	"was in a percussion-cap box. The first thing the beetle did was to"
"1259"	"take him by the finger. A natural fillip followed, the beetle went"
"1260"	"floundering into the aisle and lit on its back, and the hurt finger went"
"1261"	"into the boy’s mouth. The beetle lay there working its helpless legs,"
"1262"	"unable to turn over. Tom eyed it, and longed for it; but it was safe out"
"1263"	"of his reach. Other people uninterested in the sermon found relief in"
"1264"	"the beetle, and they eyed it too. Presently a vagrant poodle dog came"
"1265"	"idling along, sad at heart, lazy with the summer softness and the"
"1266"	"quiet, weary of captivity, sighing for change. He spied the beetle; the"
"1267"	"drooping tail lifted and wagged. He surveyed the prize; walked around"
"1268"	"it; smelt at it from a safe distance; walked around it again; grew"
"1269"	"bolder, and took a closer smell; then lifted his lip and made a gingerly"
"1270"	"snatch at it, just missing it; made another, and another; began to enjoy"
"1271"	"the diversion; subsided to his stomach with the beetle between his paws,"
"1272"	"and continued his experiments; grew weary at last, and then indifferent"
"1273"	"and absent-minded. His head nodded, and little by little his chin"
"1274"	"descended and touched the enemy, who seized it. There was a sharp yelp,"
"1275"	"a flirt of the poodle’s head, and the beetle fell a couple of yards"
"1276"	"away, and lit on its back once more. The neighboring spectators"
"1277"	"shook with a gentle inward joy, several faces went behind fans and"
"1278"	"hand-kerchiefs, and Tom was entirely happy. The dog looked foolish,"
"1279"	"and probably felt so; but there was resentment in his heart, too, and a"
"1280"	"craving for revenge. So he went to the beetle and began a wary attack on"
"1281"	"it again; jumping at it from every point of a circle, lighting with his"
"1282"	"fore-paws within an inch of the creature, making even closer snatches at"
"1283"	"it with his teeth, and jerking his head till his ears flapped again. But"
"1284"	"he grew tired once more, after a while; tried to amuse himself with a"
"1285"	"fly but found no relief; followed an ant around, with his nose close"
"1286"	"to the floor, and quickly wearied of that; yawned, sighed, forgot the"
"1287"	"beetle entirely, and sat down on it. Then there was a wild yelp of agony"
"1288"	"and the poodle went sailing up the aisle; the yelps continued, and so"
"1289"	"did the dog; he crossed the house in front of the altar; he flew"
"1290"	"down the other aisle; he crossed before the doors; he clamored up the"
"1291"	"home-stretch; his anguish grew with his progress, till presently he was"
"1292"	"but a woolly comet moving in its orbit with the gleam and the speed of"
"1293"	"light. At last the frantic sufferer sheered from its course, and sprang"
"1294"	"into its master’s lap; he flung it out of the window, and the voice of"
"1295"	"distress quickly thinned away and died in the distance."
"1296"	"By this time the whole church was red-faced and suffocating with"
"1297"	"suppressed laughter, and the sermon had come to a dead standstill."
"1298"	"The discourse was resumed presently, but it went lame and halting, all"
"1299"	"possibility of impressiveness being at an end; for even the gravest"
"1300"	"sentiments were constantly being received with a smothered burst of"
"1301"	"unholy mirth, under cover of some remote pew-back, as if the poor parson"
"1302"	"had said a rarely facetious thing. It was a genuine relief to the whole"
"1303"	"congregation when the ordeal was over and the benediction pronounced."
"1304"	"Tom Sawyer went home quite cheerful, thinking to himself that there was"
"1305"	"some satisfaction about divine service when there was a bit of variety"
"1306"	"in it. He had but one marring thought; he was willing that the dog"
"1307"	"should play with his pinchbug, but he did not think it was upright in"
"1308"	"him to carry it off."
"1309"	"CHAPTER VI"
"1310"	"MONDAY morning found Tom Sawyer miserable. Monday morning always found"
"1311"	"him so--because it began another week’s slow suffering in school. He"
"1312"	"generally began that day with wishing he had had no intervening holiday,"
"1313"	"it made the going into captivity and fetters again so much more odious."
"1314"	"Tom lay thinking. Presently it occurred to him that he wished he was"
"1315"	"sick; then he could stay home from school. Here was a vague possibility."
"1316"	"He canvassed his system. No ailment was found, and he investigated"
"1317"	"again. This time he thought he could detect colicky symptoms, and he"
"1318"	"began to encourage them with considerable hope. But they soon grew"
"1319"	"feeble, and presently died wholly away. He reflected further. Suddenly"
"1320"	"he discovered something. One of his upper front teeth was loose. This"
"1321"	"was lucky; he was about to begin to groan, as a “starter,” as he"
"1322"	"called it, when it occurred to him that if he came into court with that"
"1323"	"argument, his aunt would pull it out, and that would hurt. So he thought"
"1324"	"he would hold the tooth in reserve for the present, and seek further."
"1325"	"Nothing offered for some little time, and then he remembered hearing"
"1326"	"the doctor tell about a certain thing that laid up a patient for two or"
"1327"	"three weeks and threatened to make him lose a finger. So the boy eagerly"
"1328"	"drew his sore toe from under the sheet and held it up for inspection."
"1329"	"But now he did not know the necessary symptoms. However, it seemed"
"1330"	"well worth while to chance it, so he fell to groaning with considerable"
"1331"	"spirit."
"1332"	"But Sid slept on unconscious."
"1333"	"Tom groaned louder, and fancied that he began to feel pain in the toe."
"1334"	"No result from Sid."
"1335"	"Tom was panting with his exertions by this time. He took a rest and then"
"1336"	"swelled himself up and fetched a succession of admirable groans."
"1337"	"Sid snored on."
"1338"	"Tom was aggravated. He said, “Sid, Sid!” and shook him. This course"
"1339"	"worked well, and Tom began to groan again. Sid yawned, stretched, then"
"1340"	"brought himself up on his elbow with a snort, and began to stare at Tom."
"1341"	"Tom went on groaning. Sid said:"
"1342"	"“Tom! Say, Tom!” [No response.] “Here, Tom! TOM! What is the matter,"
"1343"	"Tom?” And he shook him and looked in his face anxiously."
"1344"	"Tom moaned out:"
"1345"	"“Oh, don’t, Sid. Don’t joggle me.”"
"1346"	"“Why, what’s the matter, Tom? I must call auntie.”"
"1347"	"“No--never mind. It’ll be over by and by, maybe. Don’t call anybody.”"
"1348"	"“But I must! _Don’t_ groan so, Tom, it’s awful. How long you been this"
"1349"	"way?”"
"1350"	"“Hours. Ouch! Oh, don’t stir so, Sid, you’ll kill me.”"
"1351"	"“Tom, why didn’t you wake me sooner? Oh, Tom, _don’t!_ It makes my flesh"
"1352"	"crawl to hear you. Tom, what is the matter?”"
"1353"	"“I forgive you everything, Sid. [Groan.] Everything you’ve ever done to"
"1354"	"me. When I’m gone--”"
"1355"	"“Oh, Tom, you ain’t dying, are you? Don’t, Tom--oh, don’t. Maybe--”"
"1356"	"“I forgive everybody, Sid. [Groan.] Tell ‘em so, Sid. And Sid, you give"
"1357"	"my window-sash and my cat with one eye to that new girl that’s come to"
"1358"	"town, and tell her--”"
"1359"	"But Sid had snatched his clothes and gone. Tom was suffering in reality,"
"1360"	"now, so handsomely was his imagination working, and so his groans had"
"1361"	"gathered quite a genuine tone."
"1362"	"Sid flew downstairs and said:"
"1363"	"“Oh, Aunt Polly, come! Tom’s dying!”"
"1364"	"“Dying!”"
"1365"	"“Yes’m. Don’t wait--come quick!”"
"1366"	"“Rubbage! I don’t believe it!”"
"1367"	"But she fled upstairs, nevertheless, with Sid and Mary at her heels."
"1368"	"And her face grew white, too, and her lip trembled. When she reached the"
"1369"	"bedside she gasped out:"
"1370"	"“You, Tom! Tom, what’s the matter with you?”"
"1371"	"“Oh, auntie, I’m--”"
"1372"	"“What’s the matter with you--what is the matter with you, child?”"
"1373"	"“Oh, auntie, my sore toe’s mortified!”"
"1374"	"The old lady sank down into a chair and laughed a little, then cried a"
"1375"	"little, then did both together. This restored her and she said:"
"1376"	"“Tom, what a turn you did give me. Now you shut up that nonsense and"
"1377"	"climb out of this.”"
"1378"	"The groans ceased and the pain vanished from the toe. The boy felt a"
"1379"	"little foolish, and he said:"
"1380"	"“Aunt Polly, it _seemed_ mortified, and it hurt so I never minded my"
"1381"	"tooth at all.”"
"1382"	"“Your tooth, indeed! What’s the matter with your tooth?”"
"1383"	"“One of them’s loose, and it aches perfectly awful.”"
"1384"	"“There, there, now, don’t begin that groaning again. Open your mouth."
"1385"	"Well--your tooth _is_ loose, but you’re not going to die about that."
"1386"	"Mary, get me a silk thread, and a chunk of fire out of the kitchen.”"
"1387"	"Tom said:"
