"1388"	"“Oh, please, auntie, don’t pull it out. It don’t hurt any more. I wish"
"1389"	"I may never stir if it does. Please don’t, auntie. I don’t want to stay"
"1390"	"home from school.”"
"1391"	"“Oh, you don’t, don’t you? So all this row was because you thought you’d"
"1392"	"get to stay home from school and go a-fishing? Tom, Tom, I love you so,"
"1393"	"and you seem to try every way you can to break my old heart with your"
"1394"	"outrageousness.” By this time the dental instruments were ready. The old"
"1395"	"lady made one end of the silk thread fast to Tom’s tooth with a loop"
"1396"	"and tied the other to the bedpost. Then she seized the chunk of fire and"
"1397"	"suddenly thrust it almost into the boy’s face. The tooth hung dangling"
"1398"	"by the bedpost, now."
"1399"	"But all trials bring their compensations. As Tom wended to school after"
"1400"	"breakfast, he was the envy of every boy he met because the gap in his"
"1401"	"upper row of teeth enabled him to expectorate in a new and admirable"
"1402"	"way. He gathered quite a following of lads interested in the exhibition;"
"1403"	"and one that had cut his finger and had been a centre of fascination and"
"1404"	"homage up to this time, now found himself suddenly without an adherent,"
"1405"	"and shorn of his glory. His heart was heavy, and he said with a disdain"
"1406"	"which he did not feel that it wasn’t anything to spit like Tom Sawyer;"
"1407"	"but another boy said, “Sour grapes!” and he wandered away a dismantled"
"1408"	"hero."
"1409"	"Shortly Tom came upon the juvenile pariah of the village, Huckleberry"
"1410"	"Finn, son of the town drunkard. Huckleberry was cordially hated and"
"1411"	"dreaded by all the mothers of the town, because he was idle and lawless"
"1412"	"and vulgar and bad--and because all their children admired him so, and"
"1413"	"delighted in his forbidden society, and wished they dared to be like"
"1414"	"him. Tom was like the rest of the respectable boys, in that he envied"
"1415"	"Huckleberry his gaudy outcast condition, and was under strict orders"
"1416"	"not to play with him. So he played with him every time he got a chance."
"1417"	"Huckleberry was always dressed in the cast-off clothes of full-grown"
"1418"	"men, and they were in perennial bloom and fluttering with rags. His hat"
"1419"	"was a vast ruin with a wide crescent lopped out of its brim; his coat,"
"1420"	"when he wore one, hung nearly to his heels and had the rearward buttons"
"1421"	"far down the back; but one suspender supported his trousers; the seat of"
"1422"	"the trousers bagged low and contained nothing, the fringed legs dragged"
"1423"	"in the dirt when not rolled up."
"1424"	"Huckleberry came and went, at his own free will. He slept on doorsteps"
"1425"	"in fine weather and in empty hogsheads in wet; he did not have to go to"
"1426"	"school or to church, or call any being master or obey anybody; he could"
"1427"	"go fishing or swimming when and where he chose, and stay as long as it"
"1428"	"suited him; nobody forbade him to fight; he could sit up as late as he"
"1429"	"pleased; he was always the first boy that went barefoot in the spring"
"1430"	"and the last to resume leather in the fall; he never had to wash, nor"
"1431"	"put on clean clothes; he could swear wonderfully. In a word, everything"
"1432"	"that goes to make life precious that boy had. So thought every harassed,"
"1433"	"hampered, respectable boy in St. Petersburg."
"1434"	"Tom hailed the romantic outcast:"
"1435"	"“Hello, Huckleberry!”"
"1436"	"“Hello yourself, and see how you like it.”"
"1437"	"“What’s that you got?”"
"1438"	"“Dead cat.”"
"1439"	"“Lemme see him, Huck. My, he’s pretty stiff. Where’d you get him?”"
"1440"	"“Bought him off’n a boy.”"
"1441"	"“What did you give?”"
"1442"	"“I give a blue ticket and a bladder that I got at the slaughter-house.”"
"1443"	"“Where’d you get the blue ticket?”"
"1444"	"“Bought it off’n Ben Rogers two weeks ago for a hoop-stick.”"
"1445"	"“Say--what is dead cats good for, Huck?”"
"1446"	"“Good for? Cure warts with.”"
"1447"	"“No! Is that so? I know something that’s better.”"
"1448"	"“I bet you don’t. What is it?”"
"1449"	"“Why, spunk-water.”"
"1450"	"“Spunk-water! I wouldn’t give a dern for spunk-water.”"
"1451"	"“You wouldn’t, wouldn’t you? D’you ever try it?”"
"1452"	"“No, I hain’t. But Bob Tanner did.”"
"1453"	"“Who told you so!”"
"1454"	"“Why, he told Jeff Thatcher, and Jeff told Johnny Baker, and Johnny"
"1455"	"told Jim Hollis, and Jim told Ben Rogers, and Ben told a nigger, and the"
"1456"	"nigger told me. There now!”"
"1457"	"“Well, what of it? They’ll all lie. Leastways all but the nigger. I"
"1458"	"don’t know _him_. But I never see a nigger that _wouldn’t_ lie. Shucks!"
"1459"	"Now you tell me how Bob Tanner done it, Huck.”"
"1460"	"“Why, he took and dipped his hand in a rotten stump where the rain-water"
"1461"	"was.”"
"1462"	"“In the daytime?”"
"1463"	"“Certainly.”"
"1464"	"“With his face to the stump?”"
"1465"	"“Yes. Least I reckon so.”"
"1466"	"“Did he say anything?”"
"1467"	"“I don’t reckon he did. I don’t know.”"
"1468"	"“Aha! Talk about trying to cure warts with spunk-water such a blame fool"
"1469"	"way as that! Why, that ain’t a-going to do any good. You got to go all"
"1470"	"by yourself, to the middle of the woods, where you know there’s a"
"1471"	"spunk-water stump, and just as it’s midnight you back up against the stump"
"1472"	"and jam your hand in and say:"
"1473"	"‘Barley-corn, barley-corn, injun-meal shorts, Spunk-water, spunk-water,"
"1474"	"swaller these warts,’"
"1475"	"and then walk away quick, eleven steps, with your eyes shut, and then"
"1476"	"turn around three times and walk home without speaking to anybody."
"1477"	"Because if you speak the charm’s busted.”"
"1478"	"“Well, that sounds like a good way; but that ain’t the way Bob Tanner"
"1479"	"done.”"
"1480"	"“No, sir, you can bet he didn’t, becuz he’s the wartiest boy in this"
"1481"	"town; and he wouldn’t have a wart on him if he’d knowed how to work"
"1482"	"spunk-water. I’ve took off thousands of warts off of my hands that way,"
"1483"	"Huck. I play with frogs so much that I’ve always got considerable many"
"1484"	"warts. Sometimes I take ‘em off with a bean.”"
"1485"	"“Yes, bean’s good. I’ve done that.”"
"1486"	"“Have you? What’s your way?”"
"1487"	"“You take and split the bean, and cut the wart so as to get some blood,"
"1488"	"and then you put the blood on one piece of the bean and take and dig"
"1489"	"a hole and bury it ‘bout midnight at the crossroads in the dark of the"
"1490"	"moon, and then you burn up the rest of the bean. You see that piece"
"1491"	"that’s got the blood on it will keep drawing and drawing, trying to"
"1492"	"fetch the other piece to it, and so that helps the blood to draw the"
"1493"	"wart, and pretty soon off she comes.”"
"1494"	"“Yes, that’s it, Huck--that’s it; though when you’re burying it if you"
"1495"	"say ‘Down bean; off wart; come no more to bother me!’ it’s better."
"1496"	"That’s the way Joe Harper does, and he’s been nearly to Coonville and"
"1497"	"most everywheres. But say--how do you cure ‘em with dead cats?”"
"1498"	"“Why, you take your cat and go and get in the grave-yard ‘long about"
"1499"	"midnight when somebody that was wicked has been buried; and when it’s"
"1500"	"midnight a devil will come, or maybe two or three, but you can’t see"
"1501"	"‘em, you can only hear something like the wind, or maybe hear ‘em talk;"
"1502"	"and when they’re taking that feller away, you heave your cat after ‘em"
"1503"	"and say, ‘Devil follow corpse, cat follow devil, warts follow cat, I’m"
"1504"	"done with ye!’ That’ll fetch _any_ wart.”"
"1505"	"“Sounds right. D’you ever try it, Huck?”"
"1506"	"“No, but old Mother Hopkins told me.”"
"1507"	"“Well, I reckon it’s so, then. Becuz they say she’s a witch.”"
"1508"	"“Say! Why, Tom, I _know_ she is. She witched pap. Pap says so his own"
"1509"	"self. He come along one day, and he see she was a-witching him, so he"
"1510"	"took up a rock, and if she hadn’t dodged, he’d a got her. Well, that"
"1511"	"very night he rolled off’n a shed wher’ he was a layin drunk, and broke"
"1512"	"his arm.”"
"1513"	"“Why, that’s awful. How did he know she was a-witching him?”"
"1514"	"“Lord, pap can tell, easy. Pap says when they keep looking at you right"
"1515"	"stiddy, they’re a-witching you. Specially if they mumble. Becuz when"
"1516"	"they mumble they’re saying the Lord’s Prayer backards.”"
"1517"	"“Say, Hucky, when you going to try the cat?”"
"1518"	"“To-night. I reckon they’ll come after old Hoss Williams to-night.”"
"1519"	"“But they buried him Saturday. Didn’t they get him Saturday night?”"
"1520"	"“Why, how you talk! How could their charms work till midnight?--and"
"1521"	"_then_ it’s Sunday. Devils don’t slosh around much of a Sunday, I don’t"
"1522"	"reckon.”"
"1523"	"“I never thought of that. That’s so. Lemme go with you?”"
"1524"	"“Of course--if you ain’t afeard.”"
"1525"	"“Afeard! ‘Tain’t likely. Will you meow?”"
"1526"	"“Yes--and you meow back, if you get a chance. Last time, you kep’ me"
"1527"	"a-meowing around till old Hays went to throwing rocks at me and says"
"1528"	"‘Dern that cat!’ and so I hove a brick through his window--but don’t you"
"1529"	"tell.”"
"1530"	"“I won’t. I couldn’t meow that night, becuz auntie was watching me, but"
"1531"	"I’ll meow this time. Say--what’s that?”"
"1532"	"“Nothing but a tick.”"
"1533"	"“Where’d you get him?”"
"1534"	"“Out in the woods.”"
"1535"	"“What’ll you take for him?”"
"1536"	"“I don’t know. I don’t want to sell him.”"
"1537"	"“All right. It’s a mighty small tick, anyway.”"
"1538"	"“Oh, anybody can run a tick down that don’t belong to them. I’m"
"1539"	"satisfied with it. It’s a good enough tick for me.”"
"1540"	"“Sho, there’s ticks a plenty. I could have a thousand of ‘em if I wanted"
"1541"	"to.”"
"1542"	"“Well, why don’t you? Becuz you know mighty well you can’t. This is a"
"1543"	"pretty early tick, I reckon. It’s the first one I’ve seen this year.”"
"1544"	"“Say, Huck--I’ll give you my tooth for him.”"
"1545"	"“Less see it.”"
"1546"	"Tom got out a bit of paper and carefully unrolled it. Huckleberry viewed"
"1547"	"it wistfully. The temptation was very strong. At last he said:"
"1548"	"“Is it genuwyne?”"
"1549"	"Tom lifted his lip and showed the vacancy."
"1550"	"“Well, all right,” said Huckleberry, “it’s a trade.”"
"1551"	"Tom enclosed the tick in the percussion-cap box that had lately been the"
"1552"	"pinchbug’s prison, and the boys separated, each feeling wealthier than"
"1553"	"before."
"1554"	"When Tom reached the little isolated frame school-house, he strode in"
"1555"	"briskly, with the manner of one who had come with all honest speed. He"
"1556"	"hung his hat on a peg and flung himself into his seat with business-like"
"1557"	"alacrity. The master, throned on high in his great splint-bottom"
"1558"	"arm-chair, was dozing, lulled by the drowsy hum of study. The"
"1559"	"interruption roused him."
"1560"	"“Thomas Sawyer!”"
"1561"	"Tom knew that when his name was pronounced in full, it meant trouble."
"1562"	"“Sir!”"
"1563"	"“Come up here. Now, sir, why are you late again, as usual?”"
"1564"	"Tom was about to take refuge in a lie, when he saw two long tails of"
"1565"	"yellow hair hanging down a back that he recognized by the electric"
"1566"	"sympathy of love; and by that form was _the only vacant place_ on the"
"1567"	"girls’ side of the school-house. He instantly said:"
"1568"	"“_I stopped to talk with Huckleberry Finn!_”"
"1569"	"The master’s pulse stood still, and he stared helplessly. The buzz of"
"1570"	"study ceased. The pupils wondered if this foolhardy boy had lost his"
"1571"	"mind. The master said:"
"1572"	"“You--you did what?”"
"1573"	"“Stopped to talk with Huckleberry Finn.”"
"1574"	"There was no mistaking the words."
"1575"	"“Thomas Sawyer, this is the most astounding confession I have ever"
"1576"	"listened to. No mere ferule will answer for this offence. Take off your"
"1577"	"jacket.”"
"1578"	"The master’s arm performed until it was tired and the stock of switches"
"1579"	"notably diminished. Then the order followed:"
"1580"	"“Now, sir, go and sit with the girls! And let this be a warning to you.”"
"1581"	"The titter that rippled around the room appeared to abash the boy, but"
"1582"	"in reality that result was caused rather more by his worshipful awe"
"1583"	"of his unknown idol and the dread pleasure that lay in his high good"
"1584"	"fortune. He sat down upon the end of the pine bench and the girl hitched"
"1585"	"herself away from him with a toss of her head. Nudges and winks and"
"1586"	"whispers traversed the room, but Tom sat still, with his arms upon the"
"1587"	"long, low desk before him, and seemed to study his book."
"1588"	"By and by attention ceased from him, and the accustomed school murmur"
"1589"	"rose upon the dull air once more. Presently the boy began to steal"
"1590"	"furtive glances at the girl. She observed it, “made a mouth” at him"
"1591"	"and gave him the back of her head for the space of a minute. When she"
"1592"	"cautiously faced around again, a peach lay before her. She thrust it"
"1593"	"away. Tom gently put it back. She thrust it away again, but with less"
"1594"	"animosity. Tom patiently returned it to its place. Then she let it"
"1595"	"remain. Tom scrawled on his slate, “Please take it--I got more.” The"
"1596"	"girl glanced at the words, but made no sign. Now the boy began to draw"
"1597"	"something on the slate, hiding his work with his left hand. For a time"
"1598"	"the girl refused to notice; but her human curiosity presently began"
"1599"	"to manifest itself by hardly perceptible signs. The boy worked on,"
"1600"	"apparently unconscious. The girl made a sort of non-committal attempt"
"1601"	"to see, but the boy did not betray that he was aware of it. At last she"
"1602"	"gave in and hesitatingly whispered:"
"1603"	"“Let me see it.”"
"1604"	"Tom partly uncovered a dismal caricature of a house with two gable ends"
"1605"	"to it and a corkscrew of smoke issuing from the chimney. Then the girl’s"
"1606"	"interest began to fasten itself upon the work and she forgot everything"
"1607"	"else. When it was finished, she gazed a moment, then whispered:"
"1608"	"“It’s nice--make a man.”"
"1609"	"The artist erected a man in the front yard, that resembled a derrick. He"
"1610"	"could have stepped over the house; but the girl was not hypercritical;"
"1611"	"she was satisfied with the monster, and whispered:"
"1612"	"“It’s a beautiful man--now make me coming along.”"
"1613"	"Tom drew an hour-glass with a full moon and straw limbs to it and armed"
"1614"	"the spreading fingers with a portentous fan. The girl said:"
"1615"	"“It’s ever so nice--I wish I could draw.”"
"1616"	"“It’s easy,” whispered Tom, “I’ll learn you.”"
"1617"	"“Oh, will you? When?”"
"1618"	"“At noon. Do you go home to dinner?”"
"1619"	"“I’ll stay if you will.”"
"1620"	"“Good--that’s a whack. What’s your name?”"
"1621"	"“Becky Thatcher. What’s yours? Oh, I know. It’s Thomas Sawyer.”"
"1622"	"“That’s the name they lick me by. I’m Tom when I’m good. You call me"
"1623"	"Tom, will you?”"
"1624"	"“Yes.”"
"1625"	"Now Tom began to scrawl something on the slate, hiding the words from"
"1626"	"the girl. But she was not backward this time. She begged to see. Tom"
"1627"	"said:"
"1628"	"“Oh, it ain’t anything.”"
"1629"	"“Yes it is.”"
"1630"	"“No it ain’t. You don’t want to see.”"
"1631"	"“Yes I do, indeed I do. Please let me.”"
"1632"	"“You’ll tell.”"
"1633"	"“No I won’t--deed and deed and double deed won’t.”"
"1634"	"“You won’t tell anybody at all? Ever, as long as you live?”"
"1635"	"“No, I won’t ever tell _any_body. Now let me.”"
"1636"	"“Oh, _you_ don’t want to see!”"
"1637"	"“Now that you treat me so, I _will_ see.” And she put her small hand"
"1638"	"upon his and a little scuffle ensued, Tom pretending to resist in"
"1639"	"earnest but letting his hand slip by degrees till these words were"
"1640"	"revealed: “_I love you_.”"
"1641"	"“Oh, you bad thing!” And she hit his hand a smart rap, but reddened and"
"1642"	"looked pleased, nevertheless."
"1643"	"Just at this juncture the boy felt a slow, fateful grip closing on his"
"1644"	"ear, and a steady lifting impulse. In that wise he was borne across the"
"1645"	"house and deposited in his own seat, under a peppering fire of giggles"
"1646"	"from the whole school. Then the master stood over him during a few awful"
"1647"	"moments, and finally moved away to his throne without saying a word. But"
"1648"	"although Tom’s ear tingled, his heart was jubilant."
"1649"	"As the school quieted down Tom made an honest effort to study, but"
"1650"	"the turmoil within him was too great. In turn he took his place in the"
"1651"	"reading class and made a botch of it; then in the geography class and"
"1652"	"turned lakes into mountains, mountains into rivers, and rivers into"
"1653"	"continents, till chaos was come again; then in the spelling class, and"
"1654"	"got “turned down,” by a succession of mere baby words, till he brought"
"1655"	"up at the foot and yielded up the pewter medal which he had worn with"
"1656"	"ostentation for months."
"1657"	"CHAPTER VII"
"1658"	"THE harder Tom tried to fasten his mind on his book, the more his ideas"
"1659"	"wandered. So at last, with a sigh and a yawn, he gave it up. It seemed"
"1660"	"to him that the noon recess would never come. The air was utterly dead."
"1661"	"There was not a breath stirring. It was the sleepiest of sleepy days."
"1662"	"The drowsing murmur of the five and twenty studying scholars soothed"
"1663"	"the soul like the spell that is in the murmur of bees. Away off in the"
"1664"	"flaming sunshine, Cardiff Hill lifted its soft green sides through a"
"1665"	"shimmering veil of heat, tinted with the purple of distance; a few birds"
"1666"	"floated on lazy wing high in the air; no other living thing was visible"
"1667"	"but some cows, and they were asleep. Tom’s heart ached to be free, or"
"1668"	"else to have something of interest to do to pass the dreary time."
"1669"	"His hand wandered into his pocket and his face lit up with a glow of"
"1670"	"gratitude that was prayer, though he did not know it. Then furtively"
"1671"	"the percussion-cap box came out. He released the tick and put him on"
"1672"	"the long flat desk. The creature probably glowed with a gratitude that"
"1673"	"amounted to prayer, too, at this moment, but it was premature: for when"
"1674"	"he started thankfully to travel off, Tom turned him aside with a pin and"
"1675"	"made him take a new direction."
"1676"	"Tom’s bosom friend sat next him, suffering just as Tom had been, and"
"1677"	"now he was deeply and gratefully interested in this entertainment in"
"1678"	"an instant. This bosom friend was Joe Harper. The two boys were sworn"
"1679"	"friends all the week, and embattled enemies on Saturdays. Joe took a"
"1680"	"pin out of his lapel and began to assist in exercising the prisoner."
"1681"	"The sport grew in interest momently. Soon Tom said that they were"
"1682"	"interfering with each other, and neither getting the fullest benefit"
"1683"	"of the tick. So he put Joe’s slate on the desk and drew a line down the"
"1684"	"middle of it from top to bottom."
"1685"	"“Now,” said he, “as long as he is on your side you can stir him up and"
"1686"	"I’ll let him alone; but if you let him get away and get on my side,"
"1687"	"you’re to leave him alone as long as I can keep him from crossing over.”"
"1688"	"“All right, go ahead; start him up.”"
"1689"	"The tick escaped from Tom, presently, and crossed the equator. Joe"
"1690"	"harassed him awhile, and then he got away and crossed back again. This"
"1691"	"change of base occurred often. While one boy was worrying the tick with"
"1692"	"absorbing interest, the other would look on with interest as strong, the"
"1693"	"two heads bowed together over the slate, and the two souls dead to all"
"1694"	"things else. At last luck seemed to settle and abide with Joe. The"
"1695"	"tick tried this, that, and the other course, and got as excited and as"
"1696"	"anxious as the boys themselves, but time and again just as he would"
"1697"	"have victory in his very grasp, so to speak, and Tom’s fingers would"
"1698"	"be twitching to begin, Joe’s pin would deftly head him off, and keep"
"1699"	"possession. At last Tom could stand it no longer. The temptation was too"
"1700"	"strong. So he reached out and lent a hand with his pin. Joe was angry in"
"1701"	"a moment. Said he:"
"1702"	"“Tom, you let him alone.”"
"1703"	"“I only just want to stir him up a little, Joe.”"
"1704"	"“No, sir, it ain’t fair; you just let him alone.”"
"1705"	"“Blame it, I ain’t going to stir him much.”"
"1706"	"“Let him alone, I tell you.”"
"1707"	"“I won’t!”"
"1708"	"“You shall--he’s on my side of the line.”"
"1709"	"“Look here, Joe Harper, whose is that tick?”"
"1710"	"“I don’t care whose tick he is--he’s on my side of the line, and you"
"1711"	"sha’n’t touch him.”"
"1712"	"“Well, I’ll just bet I will, though. He’s my tick and I’ll do what I"
"1713"	"blame please with him, or die!”"
"1714"	"A tremendous whack came down on Tom’s shoulders, and its duplicate on"
"1715"	"Joe’s; and for the space of two minutes the dust continued to fly from"
"1716"	"the two jackets and the whole school to enjoy it. The boys had been"
"1717"	"too absorbed to notice the hush that had stolen upon the school awhile"
"1718"	"before when the master came tiptoeing down the room and stood over them."
"1719"	"He had contemplated a good part of the performance before he contributed"
"1720"	"his bit of variety to it."
"1721"	"When school broke up at noon, Tom flew to Becky Thatcher, and whispered"
"1722"	"in her ear:"
"1723"	"“Put on your bonnet and let on you’re going home; and when you get to"
"1724"	"the corner, give the rest of ‘em the slip, and turn down through the"
"1725"	"lane and come back. I’ll go the other way and come it over ‘em the same"
"1726"	"way.”"
"1727"	"So the one went off with one group of scholars, and the other with"
"1728"	"another. In a little while the two met at the bottom of the lane, and"
"1729"	"when they reached the school they had it all to themselves. Then they"
"1730"	"sat together, with a slate before them, and Tom gave Becky the pencil"
"1731"	"and held her hand in his, guiding it, and so created another surprising"
"1732"	"house. When the interest in art began to wane, the two fell to talking."
"1733"	"Tom was swimming in bliss. He said:"
"1734"	"“Do you love rats?”"
"1735"	"“No! I hate them!”"
"1736"	"“Well, I do, too--_live_ ones. But I mean dead ones, to swing round your"
"1737"	"head with a string.”"
"1738"	"“No, I don’t care for rats much, anyway. What I like is chewing-gum.”"
"1739"	"“Oh, I should say so! I wish I had some now.”"
"1740"	"“Do you? I’ve got some. I’ll let you chew it awhile, but you must give"
"1741"	"it back to me.”"
"1742"	"That was agreeable, so they chewed it turn about, and dangled their legs"
"1743"	"against the bench in excess of contentment."
"1744"	"“Was you ever at a circus?” said Tom."
"1745"	"“Yes, and my pa’s going to take me again some time, if I’m good.”"
"1746"	"“I been to the circus three or four times--lots of times. Church ain’t"
"1747"	"shucks to a circus. There’s things going on at a circus all the time."
"1748"	"I’m going to be a clown in a circus when I grow up.”"
"1749"	"“Oh, are you! That will be nice. They’re so lovely, all spotted up.”"
"1750"	"“Yes, that’s so. And they get slathers of money--most a dollar a day, Ben"
"1751"	"Rogers says. Say, Becky, was you ever engaged?”"
"1752"	"“What’s that?”"
"1753"	"“Why, engaged to be married.”"
"1754"	"“No.”"
"1755"	"“Would you like to?”"
"1756"	"“I reckon so. I don’t know. What is it like?”"
"1757"	"“Like? Why it ain’t like anything. You only just tell a boy you won’t"
"1758"	"ever have anybody but him, ever ever ever, and then you kiss and that’s"
"1759"	"all. Anybody can do it.”"
"1760"	"“Kiss? What do you kiss for?”"
"1761"	"“Why, that, you know, is to--well, they always do that.”"
"1762"	"“Everybody?”"
"1763"	"“Why, yes, everybody that’s in love with each other. Do you remember"
"1764"	"what I wrote on the slate?”"
"1765"	"“Ye--yes.”"
"1766"	"“What was it?”"
"1767"	"“I sha’n’t tell you.”"
"1768"	"“Shall I tell _you_?”"
"1769"	"“Ye--yes--but some other time.”"
"1770"	"“No, now.”"
"1771"	"“No, not now--to-morrow.”"
"1772"	"“Oh, no, _now_. Please, Becky--I’ll whisper it, I’ll whisper it ever so"
"1773"	"easy.”"
"1774"	"Becky hesitating, Tom took silence for consent, and passed his arm about"
"1775"	"her waist and whispered the tale ever so softly, with his mouth close to"
"1776"	"her ear. And then he added:"
"1777"	"“Now you whisper it to me--just the same.”"
"1778"	"She resisted, for a while, and then said:"
"1779"	"“You turn your face away so you can’t see, and then I will. But you"
"1780"	"mustn’t ever tell anybody--_will_ you, Tom? Now you won’t, _will_ you?”"
"1781"	"“No, indeed, indeed I won’t. Now, Becky.”"
"1782"	"He turned his face away. She bent timidly around till her breath stirred"
"1783"	"his curls and whispered, “I--love--you!”"
"1784"	"Then she sprang away and ran around and around the desks and benches,"
"1785"	"with Tom after her, and took refuge in a corner at last, with her little"
"1786"	"white apron to her face. Tom clasped her about her neck and pleaded:"
"1787"	"“Now, Becky, it’s all done--all over but the kiss. Don’t you be afraid"
"1788"	"of that--it ain’t anything at all. Please, Becky.” And he tugged at her"
"1789"	"apron and the hands."
"1790"	"By and by she gave up, and let her hands drop; her face, all glowing"
"1791"	"with the struggle, came up and submitted. Tom kissed the red lips and"
"1792"	"said:"
"1793"	"“Now it’s all done, Becky. And always after this, you know, you ain’t"
"1794"	"ever to love anybody but me, and you ain’t ever to marry anybody but me,"
"1795"	"ever never and forever. Will you?”"
"1796"	"“No, I’ll never love anybody but you, Tom, and I’ll never marry anybody"
"1797"	"but you--and you ain’t to ever marry anybody but me, either.”"
"1798"	"“Certainly. Of course. That’s _part_ of it. And always coming to school"
"1799"	"or when we’re going home, you’re to walk with me, when there ain’t"
"1800"	"anybody looking--and you choose me and I choose you at parties, because"
"1801"	"that’s the way you do when you’re engaged.”"
"1802"	"“It’s so nice. I never heard of it before.”"
"1803"	"“Oh, it’s ever so gay! Why, me and Amy Lawrence--”"
"1804"	"The big eyes told Tom his blunder and he stopped, confused."
"1805"	"“Oh, Tom! Then I ain’t the first you’ve ever been engaged to!”"
"1806"	"The child began to cry. Tom said:"
"1807"	"“Oh, don’t cry, Becky, I don’t care for her any more.”"
"1808"	"“Yes, you do, Tom--you know you do.”"
"1809"	"Tom tried to put his arm about her neck, but she pushed him away and"
"1810"	"turned her face to the wall, and went on crying. Tom tried again, with"
"1811"	"soothing words in his mouth, and was repulsed again. Then his pride was"
"1812"	"up, and he strode away and went outside. He stood about, restless and"
"1813"	"uneasy, for a while, glancing at the door, every now and then, hoping"
"1814"	"she would repent and come to find him. But she did not. Then he began"
"1815"	"to feel badly and fear that he was in the wrong. It was a hard struggle"
"1816"	"with him to make new advances, now, but he nerved himself to it and"
"1817"	"entered. She was still standing back there in the corner, sobbing, with"
"1818"	"her face to the wall. Tom’s heart smote him. He went to her and stood a"
"1819"	"moment, not knowing exactly how to proceed. Then he said hesitatingly:"
"1820"	"“Becky, I--I don’t care for anybody but you.”"
"1821"	"No reply--but sobs."
"1822"	"“Becky”--pleadingly. “Becky, won’t you say something?”"
"1823"	"More sobs."
"1824"	"Tom got out his chiefest jewel, a brass knob from the top of an andiron,"
"1825"	"and passed it around her so that she could see it, and said:"
"1826"	"“Please, Becky, won’t you take it?”"
"1827"	"She struck it to the floor. Then Tom marched out of the house and over"
"1828"	"the hills and far away, to return to school no more that day. Presently"
"1829"	"Becky began to suspect. She ran to the door; he was not in sight; she"
"1830"	"flew around to the play-yard; he was not there. Then she called:"
"1831"	"“Tom! Come back, Tom!”"
"1832"	"She listened intently, but there was no answer. She had no companions"
"1833"	"but silence and loneliness. So she sat down to cry again and upbraid"
"1834"	"herself; and by this time the scholars began to gather again, and she"
"1835"	"had to hide her griefs and still her broken heart and take up the cross"
"1836"	"of a long, dreary, aching afternoon, with none among the strangers about"
"1837"	"her to exchange sorrows with."
"1838"	"CHAPTER VIII"
"1839"	"TOM dodged hither and thither through lanes until he was well out of the"
"1840"	"track of returning scholars, and then fell into a moody jog. He crossed"
"1841"	"a small “branch” two or three times, because of a prevailing juvenile"
"1842"	"superstition that to cross water baffled pursuit. Half an hour later"
"1843"	"he was disappearing behind the Douglas mansion on the summit of Cardiff"
"1844"	"Hill, and the school-house was hardly distinguishable away off in the"
"1845"	"valley behind him. He entered a dense wood, picked his pathless way to"
"1846"	"the centre of it, and sat down on a mossy spot under a spreading oak."
"1847"	"There was not even a zephyr stirring; the dead noonday heat had even"
"1848"	"stilled the songs of the birds; nature lay in a trance that was broken"
"1849"	"by no sound but the occasional far-off hammering of a wood-pecker, and"
"1850"	"this seemed to render the pervading silence and sense of loneliness the"
"1851"	"more profound. The boy’s soul was steeped in melancholy; his feelings"
"1852"	"were in happy accord with his surroundings. He sat long with his elbows"
"1853"	"on his knees and his chin in his hands, meditating. It seemed to him"
"1854"	"that life was but a trouble, at best, and he more than half envied Jimmy"
"1855"	"Hodges, so lately released; it must be very peaceful, he thought, to lie"
"1856"	"and slumber and dream forever and ever, with the wind whispering through"
"1857"	"the trees and caressing the grass and the flowers over the grave, and"
"1858"	"nothing to bother and grieve about, ever any more. If he only had a"
"1859"	"clean Sunday-school record he could be willing to go, and be done with"
"1860"	"it all. Now as to this girl. What had he done? Nothing. He had meant"
"1861"	"the best in the world, and been treated like a dog--like a very dog. She"
"1862"	"would be sorry some day--maybe when it was too late. Ah, if he could only"
"1863"	"die _temporarily_!"
"1864"	"But the elastic heart of youth cannot be compressed into one constrained"
"1865"	"shape long at a time. Tom presently began to drift insensibly back into"
"1866"	"the concerns of this life again. What if he turned his back, now, and"
"1867"	"disappeared mysteriously? What if he went away--ever so far away, into"
"1868"	"unknown countries beyond the seas--and never came back any more! How"
"1869"	"would she feel then! The idea of being a clown recurred to him now, only"
"1870"	"to fill him with disgust. For frivolity and jokes and spotted tights"
"1871"	"were an offense, when they intruded themselves upon a spirit that was"
"1872"	"exalted into the vague august realm of the romantic. No, he would be"
"1873"	"a soldier, and return after long years, all war-worn and illustrious."
"1874"	"No--better still, he would join the Indians, and hunt buffaloes and go on"
"1875"	"the warpath in the mountain ranges and the trackless great plains of the"
"1876"	"Far West, and away in the future come back a great chief, bristling with"
"1877"	"feathers, hideous with paint, and prance into Sunday-school, some drowsy"
"1878"	"summer morning, with a blood-curdling war-whoop, and sear the eyeballs"
"1879"	"of all his companions with unappeasable envy. But no, there was"
"1880"	"something gaudier even than this. He would be a pirate! That was it!"
"1881"	"_now_ his future lay plain before him, and glowing with unimaginable"
"1882"	"splendor. How his name would fill the world, and make people shudder!"
"1883"	"How gloriously he would go plowing the dancing seas, in his long, low,"
"1884"	"black-hulled racer, the Spirit of the Storm, with his grisly flag flying"
"1885"	"at the fore! And at the zenith of his fame, how he would suddenly appear"
"1886"	"at the old village and stalk into church, brown and weather-beaten, in"
"1887"	"his black velvet doublet and trunks, his great jack-boots, his crimson"
"1888"	"sash, his belt bristling with horse-pistols, his crime-rusted cutlass"
"1889"	"at his side, his slouch hat with waving plumes, his black flag unfurled,"
"1890"	"with the skull and crossbones on it, and hear with swelling ecstasy"
"1891"	"the whisperings, “It’s Tom Sawyer the Pirate!--the Black Avenger of the"
"1892"	"Spanish Main!”"
"1893"	"Yes, it was settled; his career was determined. He would run away from"
"1894"	"home and enter upon it. He would start the very next morning. Therefore"
"1895"	"he must now begin to get ready. He would collect his resources together."
"1896"	"He went to a rotten log near at hand and began to dig under one end of"
"1897"	"it with his Barlow knife. He soon struck wood that sounded hollow. He"
"1898"	"put his hand there and uttered this incantation impressively:"
"1899"	"“What hasn’t come here, come! What’s here, stay here!”"
"1900"	"Then he scraped away the dirt, and exposed a pine shingle. He took it"
"1901"	"up and disclosed a shapely little treasure-house whose bottom and sides"
"1902"	"were of shingles. In it lay a marble. Tom’s astonishment was bound-less!"
"1903"	"He scratched his head with a perplexed air, and said:"
"1904"	"“Well, that beats anything!”"
"1905"	"Then he tossed the marble away pettishly, and stood cogitating. The"
"1906"	"truth was, that a superstition of his had failed, here, which he and"
"1907"	"all his comrades had always looked upon as infallible. If you buried"
"1908"	"a marble with certain necessary incantations, and left it alone a"
"1909"	"fortnight, and then opened the place with the incantation he had just"
"1910"	"used, you would find that all the marbles you had ever lost had gathered"
"1911"	"themselves together there, meantime, no matter how widely they had been"
"1912"	"separated. But now, this thing had actually and unquestionably failed."
"1913"	"Tom’s whole structure of faith was shaken to its foundations. He had"
"1914"	"many a time heard of this thing succeeding but never of its failing"
"1915"	"before. It did not occur to him that he had tried it several times"
"1916"	"before, himself, but could never find the hiding-places afterward. He"
"1917"	"puzzled over the matter some time, and finally decided that some witch"
"1918"	"had interfered and broken the charm. He thought he would satisfy himself"
"1919"	"on that point; so he searched around till he found a small sandy spot"
"1920"	"with a little funnel-shaped depression in it. He laid himself down and"
"1921"	"put his mouth close to this depression and called--"
"1922"	"“Doodle-bug, doodle-bug, tell me what I want to know! Doodle-bug,"
"1923"	"doodle-bug, tell me what I want to know!”"
"1924"	"The sand began to work, and presently a small black bug appeared for a"
"1925"	"second and then darted under again in a fright."
"1926"	"“He dasn’t tell! So it _was_ a witch that done it. I just knowed it.”"
"1927"	"He well knew the futility of trying to contend against witches, so he"
"1928"	"gave up discouraged. But it occurred to him that he might as well have"
"1929"	"the marble he had just thrown away, and therefore he went and made a"
"1930"	"patient search for it. But he could not find it. Now he went back to his"
"1931"	"treasure-house and carefully placed himself just as he had been standing"
"1932"	"when he tossed the marble away; then he took another marble from his"
"1933"	"pocket and tossed it in the same way, saying:"
"1934"	"“Brother, go find your brother!”"
"1935"	"He watched where it stopped, and went there and looked. But it must"
"1936"	"have fallen short or gone too far; so he tried twice more. The last"
"1937"	"repetition was successful. The two marbles lay within a foot of each"
"1938"	"other."
"1939"	"Just here the blast of a toy tin trumpet came faintly down the green"
"1940"	"aisles of the forest. Tom flung off his jacket and trousers, turned"
"1941"	"a suspender into a belt, raked away some brush behind the rotten log,"
"1942"	"disclosing a rude bow and arrow, a lath sword and a tin trumpet, and"
"1943"	"in a moment had seized these things and bounded away, barelegged,"
"1944"	"with fluttering shirt. He presently halted under a great elm, blew an"
"1945"	"answering blast, and then began to tiptoe and look warily out, this way"
"1946"	"and that. He said cautiously--to an imaginary company:"
"1947"	"“Hold, my merry men! Keep hid till I blow.”"
"1948"	"Now appeared Joe Harper, as airily clad and elaborately armed as Tom."
"1949"	"Tom called:"
"1950"	"“Hold! Who comes here into Sherwood Forest without my pass?”"
"1951"	"“Guy of Guisborne wants no man’s pass. Who art thou that--that--”"
"1952"	"“Dares to hold such language,” said Tom, prompting--for they talked “by"
"1953"	"the book,” from memory."
"1954"	"“Who art thou that dares to hold such language?”"
"1955"	"“I, indeed! I am Robin Hood, as thy caitiff carcase soon shall know.”"
"1956"	"“Then art thou indeed that famous outlaw? Right gladly will I dispute"
"1957"	"with thee the passes of the merry wood. Have at thee!”"
"1958"	"They took their lath swords, dumped their other traps on the ground,"
"1959"	"struck a fencing attitude, foot to foot, and began a grave, careful"
"1960"	"combat, “two up and two down.” Presently Tom said:"
"1961"	"“Now, if you’ve got the hang, go it lively!”"
"1962"	"So they “went it lively,” panting and perspiring with the work. By and"
"1963"	"by Tom shouted:"
"1964"	"“Fall! fall! Why don’t you fall?”"
"1965"	"“I sha’n’t! Why don’t you fall yourself? You’re getting the worst of"
"1966"	"it.”"
"1967"	"“Why, that ain’t anything. I can’t fall; that ain’t the way it is in the"
"1968"	"book. The book says, ‘Then with one back-handed stroke he slew poor Guy"
"1969"	"of Guisborne.’ You’re to turn around and let me hit you in the back.”"
"1970"	"There was no getting around the authorities, so Joe turned, received the"
"1971"	"whack and fell."
"1972"	"“Now,” said Joe, getting up, “you got to let me kill _you_. That’s"
"1973"	"fair.”"
"1974"	"“Why, I can’t do that, it ain’t in the book.”"
"1975"	"“Well, it’s blamed mean--that’s all.”"
"1976"	"“Well, say, Joe, you can be Friar Tuck or Much the miller’s son, and lam"
"1977"	"me with a quarter-staff; or I’ll be the Sheriff of Nottingham and you be"
"1978"	"Robin Hood a little while and kill me.”"
"1979"	"This was satisfactory, and so these adventures were carried out. Then"
"1980"	"Tom became Robin Hood again, and was allowed by the treacherous nun to"
"1981"	"bleed his strength away through his neglected wound. And at last Joe,"
"1982"	"representing a whole tribe of weeping outlaws, dragged him sadly forth,"
"1983"	"gave his bow into his feeble hands, and Tom said, “Where this arrow"
"1984"	"falls, there bury poor Robin Hood under the greenwood tree.” Then he"
"1985"	"shot the arrow and fell back and would have died, but he lit on a nettle"
"1986"	"and sprang up too gaily for a corpse."
"1987"	"The boys dressed themselves, hid their accoutrements, and went off"
"1988"	"grieving that there were no outlaws any more, and wondering what modern"
"1989"	"civilization could claim to have done to compensate for their loss."
"1990"	"They said they would rather be outlaws a year in Sherwood Forest than"
"1991"	"President of the United States forever."
"1992"	"CHAPTER IX"
"1993"	"AT half-past nine, that night, Tom and Sid were sent to bed, as usual."
"1994"	"They said their prayers, and Sid was soon asleep. Tom lay awake and"
"1995"	"waited, in restless impatience. When it seemed to him that it must be"
"1996"	"nearly daylight, he heard the clock strike ten! This was despair. He"
"1997"	"would have tossed and fidgeted, as his nerves demanded, but he was"
"1998"	"afraid he might wake Sid. So he lay still, and stared up into the dark."
"1999"	"Everything was dismally still. By and by, out of the stillness, little,"
"2000"	"scarcely perceptible noises began to emphasize themselves. The ticking"
"2001"	"of the clock began to bring itself into notice. Old beams began to crack"
"2002"	"mysteriously. The stairs creaked faintly. Evidently spirits were abroad."
"2003"	"A measured, muffled snore issued from Aunt Polly’s chamber. And now the"
"2004"	"tiresome chirping of a cricket that no human ingenuity could locate,"
"2005"	"began. Next the ghastly ticking of a death-watch in the wall at the"
"2006"	"bed’s head made Tom shudder--it meant that somebody’s days were numbered."
"2007"	"Then the howl of a far-off dog rose on the night air, and was answered"
"2008"	"by a fainter howl from a remoter distance. Tom was in an agony. At last"
"2009"	"he was satisfied that time had ceased and eternity begun; he began to"
"2010"	"doze, in spite of himself; the clock chimed eleven, but he did not hear"
"2011"	"it. And then there came, mingling with his half-formed dreams, a most"
"2012"	"melancholy caterwauling. The raising of a neighboring window disturbed"
"2013"	"him. A cry of “Scat! you devil!” and the crash of an empty bottle"
"2014"	"against the back of his aunt’s woodshed brought him wide awake, and a"
"2015"	"single minute later he was dressed and out of the window and creeping"
"2016"	"along the roof of the “ell” on all fours. He “meow’d” with caution once"
"2017"	"or twice, as he went; then jumped to the roof of the woodshed and thence"
"2018"	"to the ground. Huckleberry Finn was there, with his dead cat. The boys"
"2019"	"moved off and disappeared in the gloom. At the end of half an hour they"
"2020"	"were wading through the tall grass of the graveyard."
"2021"	"It was a graveyard of the old-fashioned Western kind. It was on a hill,"
"2022"	"about a mile and a half from the village. It had a crazy board fence"
"2023"	"around it, which leaned inward in places, and outward the rest of the"
"2024"	"time, but stood upright nowhere. Grass and weeds grew rank over the"
"2025"	"whole cemetery. All the old graves were sunken in, there was not a"
"2026"	"tombstone on the place; round-topped, worm-eaten boards staggered over"
"2027"	"the graves, leaning for support and finding none. “Sacred to the memory"
"2028"	"of” So-and-So had been painted on them once, but it could no longer have"
"2029"	"been read, on the most of them, now, even if there had been light."
"2030"	"A faint wind moaned through the trees, and Tom feared it might be the"
"2031"	"spirits of the dead, complaining at being disturbed. The boys talked"
"2032"	"little, and only under their breath, for the time and the place and the"
"2033"	"pervading solemnity and silence oppressed their spirits. They found the"
"2034"	"sharp new heap they were seeking, and ensconced themselves within the"
"2035"	"protection of three great elms that grew in a bunch within a few feet of"
"2036"	"the grave."
"2037"	"Then they waited in silence for what seemed a long time. The hooting of"
"2038"	"a distant owl was all the sound that troubled the dead stillness. Tom’s"
"2039"	"reflections grew oppressive. He must force some talk. So he said in a"
"2040"	"whisper:"
"2041"	"“Hucky, do you believe the dead people like it for us to be here?”"
"2042"	"Huckleberry whispered:"
"2043"	"“I wisht I knowed. It’s awful solemn like, _ain’t_ it?”"
"2044"	"“I bet it is.”"
"2045"	"There was a considerable pause, while the boys canvassed this matter"
"2046"	"inwardly. Then Tom whispered:"
"2047"	"“Say, Hucky--do you reckon Hoss Williams hears us talking?”"
"2048"	"“O’ course he does. Least his sperrit does.”"
"2049"	"Tom, after a pause:"
"2050"	"“I wish I’d said Mister Williams. But I never meant any harm. Everybody"
"2051"	"calls him Hoss.”"
"2052"	"“A body can’t be too partic’lar how they talk ‘bout these-yer dead"
"2053"	"people, Tom.”"
"2054"	"This was a damper, and conversation died again."
"2055"	"Presently Tom seized his comrade’s arm and said:"
"2056"	"“Sh!”"
"2057"	"“What is it, Tom?” And the two clung together with beating hearts."
"2058"	"“Sh! There ‘tis again! Didn’t you hear it?”"
"2059"	"“I--”"
"2060"	"“There! Now you hear it.”"
"2061"	"“Lord, Tom, they’re coming! They’re coming, sure. What’ll we do?”"
"2062"	"“I dono. Think they’ll see us?”"
"2063"	"“Oh, Tom, they can see in the dark, same as cats. I wisht I hadn’t"
"2064"	"come.”"
"2065"	"“Oh, don’t be afeard. I don’t believe they’ll bother us. We ain’t doing"
"2066"	"any harm. If we keep perfectly still, maybe they won’t notice us at"
"2067"	"all.”"
"2068"	"“I’ll try to, Tom, but, Lord, I’m all of a shiver.”"
"2069"	"“Listen!”"
"2070"	"The boys bent their heads together and scarcely breathed. A muffled"
"2071"	"sound of voices floated up from the far end of the graveyard."
"2072"	"“Look! See there!” whispered Tom. “What is it?”"
"2073"	"“It’s devil-fire. Oh, Tom, this is awful.”"
"2074"	"Some vague figures approached through the gloom, swinging an"
"2075"	"old-fashioned tin lantern that freckled the ground with innumerable"
"2076"	"little spangles of light. Presently Huckleberry whispered with a"
"2077"	"shudder:"
"2078"	"“It’s the devils sure enough. Three of ‘em! Lordy, Tom, we’re goners!"
"2079"	"Can you pray?”"
"2080"	"“I’ll try, but don’t you be afeard. They ain’t going to hurt us. ‘Now I"
"2081"	"lay me down to sleep, I--’”"
"2082"	"“Sh!”"
"2083"	"“What is it, Huck?”"
"2084"	"“They’re _humans_! One of ‘em is, anyway. One of ‘em’s old Muff Potter’s"
"2085"	"voice.”"
"2086"	"“No--‘tain’t so, is it?”"
"2087"	"“I bet I know it. Don’t you stir nor budge. He ain’t sharp enough to"
"2088"	"notice us. Drunk, the same as usual, likely--blamed old rip!”"
"2089"	"“All right, I’ll keep still. Now they’re stuck. Can’t find it. Here they"
"2090"	"come again. Now they’re hot. Cold again. Hot again. Red hot! They’re"
"2091"	"p’inted right, this time. Say, Huck, I know another o’ them voices; it’s"
"2092"	"Injun Joe.”"
"2093"	"“That’s so--that murderin’ half-breed! I’d druther they was devils a dern"
"2094"	"sight. What kin they be up to?”"
"2095"	"The whisper died wholly out, now, for the three men had reached the"
"2096"	"grave and stood within a few feet of the boys’ hiding-place."
"2097"	"“Here it is,” said the third voice; and the owner of it held the lantern"
"2098"	"up and revealed the face of young Doctor Robinson."
"2099"	"Potter and Injun Joe were carrying a handbarrow with a rope and a couple"
"2100"	"of shovels on it. They cast down their load and began to open the grave."
"2101"	"The doctor put the lantern at the head of the grave and came and sat"
"2102"	"down with his back against one of the elm trees. He was so close the"
"2103"	"boys could have touched him."
"2104"	"“Hurry, men!” he said, in a low voice; “the moon might come out at any"
"2105"	"moment.”"
"2106"	"They growled a response and went on digging. For some time there was no"
"2107"	"noise but the grating sound of the spades discharging their freight of"
"2108"	"mould and gravel. It was very monotonous. Finally a spade struck upon"
"2109"	"the coffin with a dull woody accent, and within another minute or two"
"2110"	"the men had hoisted it out on the ground. They pried off the lid with"
"2111"	"their shovels, got out the body and dumped it rudely on the ground. The"
"2112"	"moon drifted from behind the clouds and exposed the pallid face."
"2113"	"The barrow was got ready and the corpse placed on it, covered with a"
"2114"	"blanket, and bound to its place with the rope. Potter took out a large"
"2115"	"spring-knife and cut off the dangling end of the rope and then said:"
"2116"	"“Now the cussed thing’s ready, Sawbones, and you’ll just out with"
"2117"	"another five, or here she stays.”"
"2118"	"“That’s the talk!” said Injun Joe."
"2119"	"“Look here, what does this mean?” said the doctor. “You required your"
"2120"	"pay in advance, and I’ve paid you.”"
"2121"	"“Yes, and you done more than that,” said Injun Joe, approaching the"
"2122"	"doctor, who was now standing. “Five years ago you drove me away from"
"2123"	"your father’s kitchen one night, when I come to ask for something to"
"2124"	"eat, and you said I warn’t there for any good; and when I swore I’d get"
"2125"	"even with you if it took a hundred years, your father had me jailed for"
"2126"	"a vagrant. Did you think I’d forget? The Injun blood ain’t in me for"
"2127"	"nothing. And now I’ve _got_ you, and you got to _settle_, you know!”"
"2128"	"He was threatening the doctor, with his fist in his face, by this time."
"2129"	"The doctor struck out suddenly and stretched the ruffian on the ground."
"2130"	"Potter dropped his knife, and exclaimed:"
"2131"	"“Here, now, don’t you hit my pard!” and the next moment he had grappled"
"2132"	"with the doctor and the two were struggling with might and main,"
"2133"	"trampling the grass and tearing the ground with their heels. Injun Joe"
"2134"	"sprang to his feet, his eyes flaming with passion, snatched up Potter’s"
"2135"	"knife, and went creeping, catlike and stooping, round and round about"
"2136"	"the combatants, seeking an opportunity. All at once the doctor flung"
"2137"	"himself free, seized the heavy headboard of Williams’ grave and felled"
"2138"	"Potter to the earth with it--and in the same instant the half-breed saw"
"2139"	"his chance and drove the knife to the hilt in the young man’s breast. He"
"2140"	"reeled and fell partly upon Potter, flooding him with his blood, and in"
"2141"	"the same moment the clouds blotted out the dreadful spectacle and the"
"2142"	"two frightened boys went speeding away in the dark."
"2143"	"Presently, when the moon emerged again, Injun Joe was standing over the"
"2144"	"two forms, contemplating them. The doctor murmured inarticulately, gave"
"2145"	"a long gasp or two and was still. The half-breed muttered:"
"2146"	"“_That_ score is settled--damn you.”"
"2147"	"Then he robbed the body. After which he put the fatal knife in Potter’s"
"2148"	"open right hand, and sat down on the dismantled coffin. Three--four--five"
"2149"	"minutes passed, and then Potter began to stir and moan. His hand closed"
"2150"	"upon the knife; he raised it, glanced at it, and let it fall, with a"
"2151"	"shudder. Then he sat up, pushing the body from him, and gazed at it, and"
"2152"	"then around him, confusedly. His eyes met Joe’s."
"2153"	"“Lord, how is this, Joe?” he said."
"2154"	"“It’s a dirty business,” said Joe, without moving."
"2155"	"“What did you do it for?”"
"2156"	"“I! I never done it!”"
"2157"	"“Look here! That kind of talk won’t wash.”"
"2158"	"Potter trembled and grew white."
"2159"	"“I thought I’d got sober. I’d no business to drink to-night. But it’s"
"2160"	"in my head yet--worse’n when we started here. I’m all in a muddle;"
"2161"	"can’t recollect anything of it, hardly. Tell me, Joe--_honest_, now,"
"2162"	"old feller--did I do it? Joe, I never meant to--‘pon my soul and honor, I"
"2163"	"never meant to, Joe. Tell me how it was, Joe. Oh, it’s awful--and him so"
"2164"	"young and promising.”"
"2165"	"“Why, you two was scuffling, and he fetched you one with the headboard"
"2166"	"and you fell flat; and then up you come, all reeling and staggering"
"2167"	"like, and snatched the knife and jammed it into him, just as he fetched"
"2168"	"you another awful clip--and here you’ve laid, as dead as a wedge til"
"2169"	"now.”"
"2170"	"“Oh, I didn’t know what I was a-doing. I wish I may die this minute if I"
"2171"	"did. It was all on account of the whiskey and the excitement, I reckon."
"2172"	"I never used a weepon in my life before, Joe. I’ve fought, but never"
"2173"	"with weepons. They’ll all say that. Joe, don’t tell! Say you won’t tell,"
"2174"	"Joe--that’s a good feller. I always liked you, Joe, and stood up for you,"
"2175"	"too. Don’t you remember? You _won’t_ tell, _will_ you, Joe?” And the"
"2176"	"poor creature dropped on his knees before the stolid murderer, and"
"2177"	"clasped his appealing hands."
"2178"	"“No, you’ve always been fair and square with me, Muff Potter, and I"
"2179"	"won’t go back on you. There, now, that’s as fair as a man can say.”"
"2180"	"“Oh, Joe, you’re an angel. I’ll bless you for this the longest day I"
"2181"	"live.” And Potter began to cry."
"2182"	"“Come, now, that’s enough of that. This ain’t any time for blubbering."
"2183"	"You be off yonder way and I’ll go this. Move, now, and don’t leave any"
"2184"	"tracks behind you.”"
"2185"	"Potter started on a trot that quickly increased to a run. The half-breed"
"2186"	"stood looking after him. He muttered:"
"2187"	"“If he’s as much stunned with the lick and fuddled with the rum as he"
"2188"	"had the look of being, he won’t think of the knife till he’s gone so"
"2189"	"far he’ll be afraid to come back after it to such a place by"
"2190"	"himself--chicken-heart!”"
"2191"	"Two or three minutes later the murdered man, the blanketed corpse, the"
"2192"	"lidless coffin, and the open grave were under no inspection but the"
"2193"	"moon’s. The stillness was complete again, too."
"2194"	"CHAPTER X"
"2195"	"THE two boys flew on and on, toward the village, speechless with"
"2196"	"horror. They glanced backward over their shoulders from time to time,"
"2197"	"apprehensively, as if they feared they might be followed. Every stump"
"2198"	"that started up in their path seemed a man and an enemy, and made them"
"2199"	"catch their breath; and as they sped by some outlying cottages that lay"
"2200"	"near the village, the barking of the aroused watch-dogs seemed to give"
"2201"	"wings to their feet."
"2202"	"“If we can only get to the old tannery before we break down!” whispered"
"2203"	"Tom, in short catches between breaths. “I can’t stand it much longer.”"
"2204"	"Huckleberry’s hard pantings were his only reply, and the boys fixed"
"2205"	"their eyes on the goal of their hopes and bent to their work to win it."
"2206"	"They gained steadily on it, and at last, breast to breast, they burst"
"2207"	"through the open door and fell grateful and exhausted in the sheltering"
"2208"	"shadows beyond. By and by their pulses slowed down, and Tom whispered:"
"2209"	"“Huckleberry, what do you reckon’ll come of this?”"
"2210"	"“If Doctor Robinson dies, I reckon hanging’ll come of it.”"
"2211"	"“Do you though?”"
"2212"	"“Why, I _know_ it, Tom.”"
"2213"	"Tom thought a while, then he said:"
"2214"	"“Who’ll tell? We?”"
"2215"	"“What are you talking about? S’pose something happened and Injun Joe"
"2216"	"_didn’t_ hang? Why, he’d kill us some time or other, just as dead sure"
"2217"	"as we’re a laying here.”"
"2218"	"“That’s just what I was thinking to myself, Huck.”"
"2219"	"“If anybody tells, let Muff Potter do it, if he’s fool enough. He’s"
"2220"	"generally drunk enough.”"
"2221"	"Tom said nothing--went on thinking. Presently he whispered:"
"2222"	"“Huck, Muff Potter don’t know it. How can he tell?”"
"2223"	"“What’s the reason he don’t know it?”"
"2224"	"“Because he’d just got that whack when Injun Joe done it. D’you reckon"
"2225"	"he could see anything? D’you reckon he knowed anything?”"
"2226"	"“By hokey, that’s so, Tom!”"
"2227"	"“And besides, look-a-here--maybe that whack done for _him_!”"
"2228"	"“No, ‘taint likely, Tom. He had liquor in him; I could see that; and"
"2229"	"besides, he always has. Well, when pap’s full, you might take and belt"
"2230"	"him over the head with a church and you couldn’t phase him. He says so,"
"2231"	"his own self. So it’s the same with Muff Potter, of course. But if a man"
"2232"	"was dead sober, I reckon maybe that whack might fetch him; I dono.”"
"2233"	"After another reflective silence, Tom said:"
"2234"	"“Hucky, you sure you can keep mum?”"
"2235"	"“Tom, we _got_ to keep mum. You know that. That Injun devil wouldn’t"
"2236"	"make any more of drownding us than a couple of cats, if we was to squeak"
"2237"	"‘bout this and they didn’t hang him. Now, look-a-here, Tom, less take"
"2238"	"and swear to one another--that’s what we got to do--swear to keep mum.”"
"2239"	"“I’m agreed. It’s the best thing. Would you just hold hands and swear"
"2240"	"that we--”"
"2241"	"“Oh no, that wouldn’t do for this. That’s good enough for little"
"2242"	"rubbishy common things--specially with gals, cuz _they_ go back on you"
"2243"	"anyway, and blab if they get in a huff--but there orter be writing ‘bout"
"2244"	"a big thing like this. And blood.”"
"2245"	"Tom’s whole being applauded this idea. It was deep, and dark, and awful;"
"2246"	"the hour, the circumstances, the surroundings, were in keeping with it."
"2247"	"He picked up a clean pine shingle that lay in the moon-light, took a"
"2248"	"little fragment of “red keel” out of his pocket, got the moon on"
"2249"	"his work, and painfully scrawled these lines, emphasizing each slow"
"2250"	"down-stroke by clamping his tongue between his teeth, and letting up the"
"2251"	"pressure on the up-strokes. [See next page.]"
"2252"	"“Huck Finn and Tom Sawyer swears they will keep mum about This and They"
"2253"	"wish They may Drop down dead in Their Tracks if They ever Tell and Rot.”"
"2254"	"Huckleberry was filled with admiration of Tom’s facility in writing, and"
"2255"	"the sublimity of his language. He at once took a pin from his lapel and"
"2256"	"was going to prick his flesh, but Tom said:"
"2257"	"“Hold on! Don’t do that. A pin’s brass. It might have verdigrease on"
"2258"	"it.”"
"2259"	"“What’s verdigrease?”"
"2260"	"“It’s p’ison. That’s what it is. You just swaller some of it once--you’ll"
"2261"	"see.”"
"2262"	"So Tom unwound the thread from one of his needles, and each boy pricked"
"2263"	"the ball of his thumb and squeezed out a drop of blood. In time, after"
"2264"	"many squeezes, Tom managed to sign his initials, using the ball of his"
"2265"	"little finger for a pen. Then he showed Huckleberry how to make an H and"
"2266"	"an F, and the oath was complete. They buried the shingle close to the"
"2267"	"wall, with some dismal ceremonies and incantations, and the fetters"
"2268"	"that bound their tongues were considered to be locked and the key thrown"
"2269"	"away."
"2270"	"A figure crept stealthily through a break in the other end of the ruined"
"2271"	"building, now, but they did not notice it."
"2272"	"“Tom,” whispered Huckleberry, “does this keep us from _ever_"
"2273"	"telling--_always_?”"
"2274"	"“Of course it does. It don’t make any difference _what_ happens, we got"
"2275"	"to keep mum. We’d drop down dead--don’t _you_ know that?”"
"2276"	"“Yes, I reckon that’s so.”"
"2277"	"They continued to whisper for some little time. Presently a dog set up"
"2278"	"a long, lugubrious howl just outside--within ten feet of them. The boys"
"2279"	"clasped each other suddenly, in an agony of fright."
"2280"	"“Which of us does he mean?” gasped Huckleberry."
"2281"	"“I dono--peep through the crack. Quick!”"
"2282"	"“No, _you_, Tom!”"
"2283"	"“I can’t--I can’t _do_ it, Huck!”"
"2284"	"“Please, Tom. There ‘tis again!”"
"2285"	"“Oh, lordy, I’m thankful!” whispered Tom. “I know his voice. It’s Bull"
"2286"	"Harbison.” *"
"2287"	"[* If Mr. Harbison owned a slave named Bull, Tom would have spoken of"
"2288"	"him as “Harbison’s Bull,” but a son or a dog of that name was “Bull"
"2289"	"Harbison.”]"
"2290"	"“Oh, that’s good--I tell you, Tom, I was most scared to death; I’d a bet"
"2291"	"anything it was a _stray_ dog.”"
"2292"	"The dog howled again. The boys’ hearts sank once more."
"2293"	"“Oh, my! that ain’t no Bull Harbison!” whispered Huckleberry. “_Do_,"
"2294"	"Tom!”"
"2295"	"Tom, quaking with fear, yielded, and put his eye to the crack. His"
"2296"	"whisper was hardly audible when he said:"
"2297"	"“Oh, Huck, _its a stray dog_!”"
"2298"	"“Quick, Tom, quick! Who does he mean?”"
"2299"	"“Huck, he must mean us both--we’re right together.”"
"2300"	"“Oh, Tom, I reckon we’re goners. I reckon there ain’t no mistake ‘bout"
"2301"	"where _I’ll_ go to. I been so wicked.”"
"2302"	"“Dad fetch it! This comes of playing hookey and doing everything a"
"2303"	"feller’s told _not_ to do. I might a been good, like Sid, if I’d a"
"2304"	"tried--but no, I wouldn’t, of course. But if ever I get off this time,"
"2305"	"I lay I’ll just _waller_ in Sunday-schools!” And Tom began to snuffle a"
"2306"	"little."
"2307"	"“_You_ bad!” and Huckleberry began to snuffle too. “Consound it, Tom"
"2308"	"Sawyer, you’re just old pie, ‘long-side o’ what I am. Oh, _lordy_,"
"2309"	"lordy, lordy, I wisht I only had half your chance.”"
"2310"	"Tom choked off and whispered:"
"2311"	"“Look, Hucky, look! He’s got his _back_ to us!”"
"2312"	"Hucky looked, with joy in his heart."
"2313"	"“Well, he has, by jingoes! Did he before?”"
"2314"	"“Yes, he did. But I, like a fool, never thought. Oh, this is bully, you"
"2315"	"know. _Now_ who can he mean?”"
"2316"	"The howling stopped. Tom pricked up his ears."
"2317"	"“Sh! What’s that?” he whispered."
"2318"	"“Sounds like--like hogs grunting. No--it’s somebody snoring, Tom.”"
"2319"	"“That _is_ it! Where ‘bouts is it, Huck?”"
"2320"	"“I bleeve it’s down at ‘tother end. Sounds so, anyway. Pap used to sleep"
"2321"	"there, sometimes, ‘long with the hogs, but laws bless you, he just lifts"
"2322"	"things when _he_ snores. Besides, I reckon he ain’t ever coming back to"
"2323"	"this town any more.”"
"2324"	"The spirit of adventure rose in the boys’ souls once more."
"2325"	"“Hucky, do you das’t to go if I lead?”"
"2326"	"“I don’t like to, much. Tom, s’pose it’s Injun Joe!”"
"2327"	"Tom quailed. But presently the temptation rose up strong again and the"
"2328"	"boys agreed to try, with the understanding that they would take to their"
"2329"	"heels if the snoring stopped. So they went tiptoeing stealthily down,"
"2330"	"the one behind the other. When they had got to within five steps of the"
"2331"	"snorer, Tom stepped on a stick, and it broke with a sharp snap. The man"
"2332"	"moaned, writhed a little, and his face came into the moonlight. It was"
"2333"	"Muff Potter. The boys’ hearts had stood still, and their hopes too,"
"2334"	"when the man moved, but their fears passed away now. They tip-toed out,"
"2335"	"through the broken weather-boarding, and stopped at a little distance"
"2336"	"to exchange a parting word. That long, lugubrious howl rose on the night"
"2337"	"air again! They turned and saw the strange dog standing within a few"
"2338"	"feet of where Potter was lying, and _facing_ Potter, with his nose"
"2339"	"pointing heavenward."
"2340"	"“Oh, geeminy, it’s _him_!” exclaimed both boys, in a breath."
"2341"	"“Say, Tom--they say a stray dog come howling around Johnny Miller’s"
"2342"	"house, ‘bout midnight, as much as two weeks ago; and a whippoorwill come"
"2343"	"in and lit on the banisters and sung, the very same evening; and there"
"2344"	"ain’t anybody dead there yet.”"
"2345"	"“Well, I know that. And suppose there ain’t. Didn’t Gracie Miller fall"
"2346"	"in the kitchen fire and burn herself terrible the very next Saturday?”"
"2347"	"“Yes, but she ain’t _dead_. And what’s more, she’s getting better, too.”"
"2348"	"“All right, you wait and see. She’s a goner, just as dead sure as Muff"
"2349"	"Potter’s a goner. That’s what the niggers say, and they know all about"
"2350"	"these kind of things, Huck.”"
"2351"	"Then they separated, cogitating. When Tom crept in at his bedroom window"
"2352"	"the night was almost spent. He undressed with excessive caution, and"
"2353"	"fell asleep congratulating himself that nobody knew of his escapade. He"
"2354"	"was not aware that the gently-snoring Sid was awake, and had been so for"
"2355"	"an hour."
"2356"	"When Tom awoke, Sid was dressed and gone. There was a late look in the"
"2357"	"light, a late sense in the atmosphere. He was startled. Why had he not"
"2358"	"been called--persecuted till he was up, as usual? The thought filled"
"2359"	"him with bodings. Within five minutes he was dressed and down-stairs,"
"2360"	"feeling sore and drowsy. The family were still at table, but they had"
"2361"	"finished breakfast. There was no voice of rebuke; but there were averted"
"2362"	"eyes; there was a silence and an air of solemnity that struck a chill"
"2363"	"to the culprit’s heart. He sat down and tried to seem gay, but it"
"2364"	"was up-hill work; it roused no smile, no response, and he lapsed into"
"2365"	"silence and let his heart sink down to the depths."
"2366"	"After breakfast his aunt took him aside, and Tom almost brightened in"
"2367"	"the hope that he was going to be flogged; but it was not so. His aunt"
"2368"	"wept over him and asked him how he could go and break her old heart so;"
"2369"	"and finally told him to go on, and ruin himself and bring her gray hairs"
"2370"	"with sorrow to the grave, for it was no use for her to try any more."
"2371"	"This was worse than a thousand whippings, and Tom’s heart was sorer now"
"2372"	"than his body. He cried, he pleaded for forgiveness, promised to reform"
"2373"	"over and over again, and then received his dismissal, feeling that"
"2374"	"he had won but an imperfect forgiveness and established but a feeble"
"2375"	"confidence."
"2376"	"He left the presence too miserable to even feel revengeful toward"
"2377"	"Sid; and so the latter’s prompt retreat through the back gate was"
"2378"	"unnecessary. He moped to school gloomy and sad, and took his flogging,"
"2379"	"along with Joe Harper, for playing hookey the day before, with the"
"2380"	"air of one whose heart was busy with heavier woes and wholly dead to"
"2381"	"trifles. Then he betook himself to his seat, rested his elbows on his"
"2382"	"desk and his jaws in his hands, and stared at the wall with the stony"
"2383"	"stare of suffering that has reached the limit and can no further go."
"2384"	"His elbow was pressing against some hard substance. After a long time"
"2385"	"he slowly and sadly changed his position, and took up this object with"
"2386"	"a sigh. It was in a paper. He unrolled it. A long, lingering, colossal"
"2387"	"sigh followed, and his heart broke. It was his brass andiron knob!"
"2388"	"This final feather broke the camel’s back."
"2389"	"CHAPTER XI"
"2390"	"CLOSE upon the hour of noon the whole village was suddenly electrified"
"2391"	"with the ghastly news. No need of the as yet un-dreamed-of telegraph;"
"2392"	"the tale flew from man to man, from group to group, from house to house,"
"2393"	"with little less than telegraphic speed. Of course the schoolmaster gave"
"2394"	"holi-day for that afternoon; the town would have thought strangely of"
"2395"	"him if he had not."
"2396"	"A gory knife had been found close to the murdered man, and it had been"
"2397"	"recognized by somebody as belonging to Muff Potter--so the story ran. And"
"2398"	"it was said that a belated citizen had come upon Potter washing himself"
"2399"	"in the “branch” about one or two o’clock in the morning, and that Potter"
"2400"	"had at once sneaked off--suspicious circumstances, especially the washing"
"2401"	"which was not a habit with Potter. It was also said that the town had"
"2402"	"been ransacked for this “murderer” (the public are not slow in the"
"2403"	"matter of sifting evidence and arriving at a verdict), but that he"
"2404"	"could not be found. Horsemen had departed down all the roads in every"
"2405"	"direction, and the Sheriff “was confident” that he would be captured"
"2406"	"before night."
"2407"	"All the town was drifting toward the graveyard. Tom’s heartbreak"
"2408"	"vanished and he joined the procession, not because he would not"
"2409"	"a thousand times rather go anywhere else, but because an awful,"
"2410"	"unaccountable fascination drew him on. Arrived at the dreadful place, he"
"2411"	"wormed his small body through the crowd and saw the dismal spectacle."
"2412"	"It seemed to him an age since he was there before. Somebody pinched"
"2413"	"his arm. He turned, and his eyes met Huckleberry’s. Then both looked"
"2414"	"elsewhere at once, and wondered if anybody had noticed anything in their"
"2415"	"mutual glance. But everybody was talking, and intent upon the grisly"
"2416"	"spectacle before them."
"2417"	"“Poor fellow!” “Poor young fellow!” “This ought to be a lesson to grave"
"2418"	"robbers!” “Muff Potter’ll hang for this if they catch him!” This was the"
"2419"	"drift of remark; and the minister said, “It was a judgment; His hand is"
"2420"	"here.”"
"2421"	"Now Tom shivered from head to heel; for his eye fell upon the stolid"
"2422"	"face of Injun Joe. At this moment the crowd began to sway and struggle,"
"2423"	"and voices shouted, “It’s him! it’s him! he’s coming himself!”"
"2424"	"“Who? Who?” from twenty voices."
"2425"	"“Muff Potter!”"
"2426"	"“Hallo, he’s stopped!--Look out, he’s turning! Don’t let him get away!”"
"2427"	"People in the branches of the trees over Tom’s head said he wasn’t"
"2428"	"trying to get away--he only looked doubtful and perplexed."
"2429"	"“Infernal impudence!” said a bystander; “wanted to come and take a quiet"
"2430"	"look at his work, I reckon--didn’t expect any company.”"
"2431"	"The crowd fell apart, now, and the Sheriff came through, ostentatiously"
"2432"	"leading Potter by the arm. The poor fellow’s face was haggard, and"
"2433"	"his eyes showed the fear that was upon him. When he stood before the"
"2434"	"murdered man, he shook as with a palsy, and he put his face in his hands"
"2435"	"and burst into tears."
"2436"	"“I didn’t do it, friends,” he sobbed; “‘pon my word and honor I never"
"2437"	"done it.”"
"2438"	"“Who’s accused you?” shouted a voice."
"2439"	"This shot seemed to carry home. Potter lifted his face and looked around"
"2440"	"him with a pathetic hopelessness in his eyes. He saw Injun Joe, and"
"2441"	"exclaimed:"
"2442"	"“Oh, Injun Joe, you promised me you’d never--”"
"2443"	"“Is that your knife?” and it was thrust before him by the Sheriff."
"2444"	"Potter would have fallen if they had not caught him and eased him to the"
"2445"	"ground. Then he said:"
"2446"	"“Something told me ‘t if I didn’t come back and get--” He shuddered; then"
"2447"	"waved his nerveless hand with a vanquished gesture and said, “Tell ‘em,"
"2448"	"Joe, tell ‘em--it ain’t any use any more.”"
"2449"	"Then Huckleberry and Tom stood dumb and staring, and heard the"
"2450"	"stony-hearted liar reel off his serene statement, they expecting every"
"2451"	"moment that the clear sky would deliver God’s lightnings upon his head,"
"2452"	"and wondering to see how long the stroke was delayed. And when he had"
"2453"	"finished and still stood alive and whole, their wavering impulse to"
"2454"	"break their oath and save the poor betrayed prisoner’s life faded and"
"2455"	"vanished away, for plainly this miscreant had sold himself to Satan and"
"2456"	"it would be fatal to meddle with the property of such a power as that."
"2457"	"“Why didn’t you leave? What did you want to come here for?” somebody"
"2458"	"said."
"2459"	"“I couldn’t help it--I couldn’t help it,” Potter moaned. “I wanted to"
"2460"	"run away, but I couldn’t seem to come anywhere but here.” And he fell to"
"2461"	"sobbing again."
"2462"	"Injun Joe repeated his statement, just as calmly, a few minutes"
"2463"	"afterward on the inquest, under oath; and the boys, seeing that the"
"2464"	"lightnings were still withheld, were confirmed in their belief that"
"2465"	"Joe had sold himself to the devil. He was now become, to them, the most"
"2466"	"balefully interesting object they had ever looked upon, and they could"
"2467"	"not take their fascinated eyes from his face."
"2468"	"They inwardly resolved to watch him nights, when opportunity should"
"2469"	"offer, in the hope of getting a glimpse of his dread master."
"2470"	"Injun Joe helped to raise the body of the murdered man and put it in"
"2471"	"a wagon for removal; and it was whispered through the shuddering"
"2472"	"crowd that the wound bled a little! The boys thought that this happy"
"2473"	"circumstance would turn suspicion in the right direction; but they were"
"2474"	"disappointed, for more than one villager remarked:"
"2475"	"“It was within three feet of Muff Potter when it done it.”"
"2476"	"Tom’s fearful secret and gnawing conscience disturbed his sleep for as"
"2477"	"much as a week after this; and at breakfast one morning Sid said:"
"2478"	"“Tom, you pitch around and talk in your sleep so much that you keep me"
"2479"	"awake half the time.”"
"2480"	"Tom blanched and dropped his eyes."
"2481"	"“It’s a bad sign,” said Aunt Polly, gravely. “What you got on your mind,"
"2482"	"Tom?”"
"2483"	"“Nothing. Nothing ‘t I know of.” But the boy’s hand shook so that he"
"2484"	"spilled his coffee."
"2485"	"“And you do talk such stuff,” Sid said. “Last night you said, ‘It’s"
"2486"	"blood, it’s blood, that’s what it is!’ You said that over and over."
"2487"	"And you said, ‘Don’t torment me so--I’ll tell!’ Tell _what_? What is it"
"2488"	"you’ll tell?”"
"2489"	"Everything was swimming before Tom. There is no telling what might have"
"2490"	"happened, now, but luckily the concern passed out of Aunt Polly’s face"
"2491"	"and she came to Tom’s relief without knowing it. She said:"
"2492"	"“Sho! It’s that dreadful murder. I dream about it most every night"
"2493"	"myself. Sometimes I dream it’s me that done it.”"
"2494"	"Mary said she had been affected much the same way. Sid seemed satisfied."
"2495"	"Tom got out of the presence as quick as he plausibly could, and after"
"2496"	"that he complained of toothache for a week, and tied up his jaws every"
"2497"	"night. He never knew that Sid lay nightly watching, and frequently"
"2498"	"slipped the bandage free and then leaned on his elbow listening a good"
"2499"	"while at a time, and afterward slipped the bandage back to its place"
"2500"	"again. Tom’s distress of mind wore off gradually and the toothache grew"
"2501"	"irksome and was discarded. If Sid really managed to make anything out of"
"2502"	"Tom’s disjointed mutterings, he kept it to himself."
"2503"	"It seemed to Tom that his schoolmates never would get done holding"
"2504"	"inquests on dead cats, and thus keeping his trouble present to his mind."
"2505"	"Sid noticed that Tom never was coroner at one of these inquiries,"
"2506"	"though it had been his habit to take the lead in all new enterprises;"
"2507"	"he noticed, too, that Tom never acted as a witness--and that was strange;"
"2508"	"and Sid did not overlook the fact that Tom even showed a marked aversion"
"2509"	"to these inquests, and always avoided them when he could. Sid marvelled,"
"2510"	"but said nothing. However, even inquests went out of vogue at last, and"
"2511"	"ceased to torture Tom’s conscience."
"2512"	"Every day or two, during this time of sorrow, Tom watched his"
"2513"	"opportunity and went to the little grated jail-window and smuggled such"
"2514"	"small comforts through to the “murderer” as he could get hold of. The"
"2515"	"jail was a trifling little brick den that stood in a marsh at the edge"
"2516"	"of the village, and no guards were afforded for it; indeed, it"
"2517"	"was seldom occupied. These offerings greatly helped to ease Tom’s"
"2518"	"conscience."
"2519"	"The villagers had a strong desire to tar-and-feather Injun Joe and ride"
"2520"	"him on a rail, for body-snatching, but so formidable was his character"
"2521"	"that nobody could be found who was willing to take the lead in the"
"2522"	"matter, so it was dropped. He had been careful to begin both of his"
"2523"	"inquest-statements with the fight, without confessing the grave-robbery"
"2524"	"that preceded it; therefore it was deemed wisest not to try the case in"
"2525"	"the courts at present."
"2526"	"CHAPTER XII"
"2527"	"ONE of the reasons why Tom’s mind had drifted away from its secret"
"2528"	"troubles was, that it had found a new and weighty matter to interest"
"2529"	"itself about. Becky Thatcher had stopped coming to school. Tom had"
"2530"	"struggled with his pride a few days, and tried to “whistle her down the"
"2531"	"wind,” but failed. He began to find himself hanging around her father’s"
"2532"	"house, nights, and feeling very miserable. She was ill. What if she"
"2533"	"should die! There was distraction in the thought. He no longer took an"
"2534"	"interest in war, nor even in piracy. The charm of life was gone; there"
"2535"	"was nothing but dreariness left. He put his hoop away, and his bat;"
"2536"	"there was no joy in them any more. His aunt was concerned. She began to"
"2537"	"try all manner of remedies on him. She was one of those people who"
"2538"	"are infatuated with patent medicines and all new-fangled methods of"
"2539"	"producing health or mending it. She was an inveterate experimenter in"
"2540"	"these things. When something fresh in this line came out she was in a"
"2541"	"fever, right away, to try it; not on herself, for she was never ailing,"
"2542"	"but on anybody else that came handy. She was a subscriber for all the"
"2543"	"“Health” periodicals and phrenological frauds; and the solemn ignorance"
"2544"	"they were inflated with was breath to her nostrils. All the “rot” they"
"2545"	"contained about ventilation, and how to go to bed, and how to get up,"
"2546"	"and what to eat, and what to drink, and how much exercise to take, and"
"2547"	"what frame of mind to keep one’s self in, and what sort of clothing"
"2548"	"to wear, was all gospel to her, and she never observed that her"
"2549"	"health-journals of the current month customarily upset everything they"
"2550"	"had recommended the month before. She was as simple-hearted and honest"
"2551"	"as the day was long, and so she was an easy victim. She gathered"
"2552"	"together her quack periodicals and her quack medicines, and thus armed"
"2553"	"with death, went about on her pale horse, metaphorically speaking, with"
"2554"	"“hell following after.” But she never suspected that she was not an"
"2555"	"angel of healing and the balm of Gilead in disguise, to the suffering"
"2556"	"neighbors."
"2557"	"The water treatment was new, now, and Tom’s low condition was a windfall"
"2558"	"to her. She had him out at daylight every morning, stood him up in the"
"2559"	"wood-shed and drowned him with a deluge of cold water; then she scrubbed"
"2560"	"him down with a towel like a file, and so brought him to; then she"
"2561"	"rolled him up in a wet sheet and put him away under blankets till she"
"2562"	"sweated his soul clean and “the yellow stains of it came through his"
"2563"	"pores”--as Tom said."
"2564"	"Yet notwithstanding all this, the boy grew more and more melancholy and"
"2565"	"pale and dejected. She added hot baths, sitz baths, shower baths, and"
"2566"	"plunges. The boy remained as dismal as a hearse. She began to assist the"
"2567"	"water with a slim oatmeal diet and blister-plasters. She calculated his"
"2568"	"capacity as she would a jug’s, and filled him up every day with quack"
"2569"	"cure-alls."
"2570"	"Tom had become indifferent to persecution by this time. This phase"
"2571"	"filled the old lady’s heart with consternation. This indifference must"
"2572"	"be broken up at any cost. Now she heard of Pain-killer for the first"
"2573"	"time. She ordered a lot at once. She tasted it and was filled with"
"2574"	"gratitude. It was simply fire in a liquid form. She dropped the water"
"2575"	"treatment and everything else, and pinned her faith to Pain-killer."
"2576"	"She gave Tom a teaspoonful and watched with the deepest anxiety for the"
"2577"	"result. Her troubles were instantly at rest, her soul at peace again;"
"2578"	"for the “indifference” was broken up. The boy could not have shown a"
